Divine Attributes
beginner
8 min
Divine Names as Attributes: Al-Ghaffār to Al-Fattāḥ
Al-Jurjani interprets five divine names with their attribute classifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani derives attribute types (sifāt) from the linguistic meanings of the divine names, using al-Ghaffār, al-Qahhār, al-Wahhāb, al-Razzāq, and al-Fattāḥ as examples.

Core Idea
A name's meaning can reveal whether it is an attribute of essence, action, or negation.

The passage opens with a series of divine names, each explained through its root and then classified as a specific type of attribute. This establishes the method of linking lexicology to theology, showing how the same name can yield different classifications based on interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: name → linguistic meaning → attribute classification.
Pay attention to the variety of attribute types: fi‘liyya, salbiyya, kalāmiyya, etc.
2
Identify the pattern
For each name, note the interpretation given and the resulting attribute type. For example, al-Qahhār is 'negative-active' because it combines dominance (active) with lack of being dominated (negative).
3
Examine multiple interpretations
Focus on al-Fattāḥ, which has several interpretations leading to different attribute types (active, speech, power/will). Reflect on why the commentator includes multiple views.
This shows the richness of the divine names.
4
Summarize the lesson
In your own words, describe how linguistic derivation determines attribute classification. Write one sentence for each name covered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغفار: أي المريد لإزالة العقوبة عن مستحقها، فهو راجع إلى صفة الإرادة واشتقاقه من الغفر بمعنى الستر. القهار: غالب لا يغلب، فهو صفة فعلية سلبية. الوهاب: كثير العطاء بلا عوض، فيكون صفة فعلية. الرزاق: يرزق من يشاء من الحيوان ما ينتفع به من مأكول ومشروب وملبوس، فهو من الفعل. الفتاح: ميسر العسير، وقيل: خالق الفتح أي النصر، وهو على التقديرين راجع إلى الصفات الفعلية. وقيل: الحاكم، وهو أي الحكم إما بالإخبار والقول فيكون صفة كلامية، أو بالقضاء والقدر فيرجع إلى صفة القدرة والإرادة. والفتاح بمعنى الحاكم مشتق من الفتاحة وهي الحكم. ومنه قوله تعالى: {ربنا افتح بيننا وبين قومنا بالحق} [الأعراف: 89] أي احكم. وقيل: الحاكم معناه المانع، ومنه حكمة اللجام وهي الحديدة المانعة من جماح الدابة، فهو صفة فعلية. العليم: العالم بالمعلومات، فهو صفة حقيقية. القابض: المختص بالسلب، الباسط: المختص بالتوسعة في العطية. الخافض: دافع البلية من الخفض وهو الحط والوضع، الرافع: المعطي للمنازل، المعز: معطي العزة وفي أكثر نسخ الكتاب معطي القوة وكلاهما ظاهر. المذل: الموجب لحط المنزلة، فهذه كلها صفة فعلية. السميع البصير ظاهر معناهما مما سبق. الحكم الحاكم وقد عرفت معناه ومرجعه. وقيل الحكم هو الصحيح علمه وقوله وفعله فيرجع إلى هذه الصفات. العدل: لا يقبح منه ما يفعل، فهو صفة سلبية. اللطيف: خالق اللطف يلطف بعباده من حيث لا يعلمون ولا يحتسبون. وقيل: العالم بالخفيات، فعلى الأول يرجع إلى الفعل وعلى الثاني إلى العلم. الخبير: معناه العليم، فصفة علمية. وقيل: المخير، فصفة كلامية. الحليم: لا يعجل العقاب للعصاة قبل وقته المقدر فيرجع إلى السلب. العظيم قد مر معناه في تفسير الجبار. الغفور كالغفار بلا فرق على قياس الرحمن الرحيم. الشكور: المجازي على الشكر فإن جزاء الشيء يسمى باسمه. وقيل: معناه أنه يثبت على القليل من الطاعة الكثير من النعمة، وعلى التقديرين هو صفة فعلية. وقيل: معناه المثني على من أطاعه فيكون صفة كلامية. العلي الكبير: هما كالمتكبر في المعنى. الحفيظ: معناه العليم من الذي هو ضد السهو والنسيان ومرجعه العلم. وقيل: من لا يشغله شيء عن شيء فمرجعه صفة سلبية. وقيل: يبقي صور الأشياء فصفة فعلية من الحفظ الذي يضاد التضييع. المقيت: خالق الأقوات وقيل: المقدر فيرجع على التقديرين إلى الفعل. وقيل: معناه الشهيد: هو العالم بالغائب والحاضر كما سيأتي في تفسيره.
الحفظ
Al-Ghaffār (the Oft-Forgiving): i.e., the One who wills the removal of punishment from those who deserve it, so it goes back to the attribute of will (irāda), and its derivation is from ghafr meaning concealment. Al-Qahhār (the Subduer): Dominant, not dominated, so it is a negative-active attribute (ṣifa fi‘liyya salbiyya). Al-Wahhāb (the Bestower): Much giving without compensation, so it becomes an active attribute (ṣifa fi‘liyya). Al-Razzāq (the Provider): He provides whomever He wills from living beings with what they benefit from of food, drink, and clothing; thus it is from act. Al-Fattāḥ (the Opener, the Judge): The One who makes the difficult easy, and it is said: the Creator of conquest (fatḥ) i.e., victory, and on both interpretations it goes back to the active attributes. And it is said: al-Ḥākim (the Judge), and it – i.e., judgment (ḥukm) – is either by informing and speech, so it becomes a speech attribute (ṣifa kalāmiyya), or by decree and destiny (qaḍā’ and qadar), so it goes back to the attribute of power and will. And al-Fattāḥ in the meaning of al-Ḥākim is derived from futāḥa which is judgment. And from this is His saying: {Our Lord, judge between us and our people with truth} [al-A‘rāf: 89], i.e., judge. And it is said: al-Ḥākim means the preventing (al-māni‘), and from this is the bit of the bridle (ḥikmat al-lijām) which is the iron that prevents the animal from bolting; thus it is an active attribute. Al-‘Alīm (the All-Knowing): the Knower of all known things, so it is a real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya). Al-Qābiḍ (the Constrictor): the One specifically characterized by withdrawal (salb), Al-Bāsiṭ (the Expander): the One specifically characterized by expansion (tawsi‘a) in giving. Al-Khāfiḍ (the Abaser): the Repeller of affliction, from khafḍ meaning lowering and placing; Al-Rāfi‘ (the Exalter): the Giver of stations; Al-Mu‘izz (the Bestower of honor): the Giver of ‘izza (honor), and in most copies of the book 'the Giver of strength' – both are apparent. Al-Mudhill (the Humiliator): the One who necessitates the lowering of station – all these are active attributes. Al-Samī‘ (the All-Hearing) and al-Baṣīr (the All-Seeing): their meanings are apparent from what preceded. Al-Ḥakam (the Judge) and al-Ḥākim (the Ruler) – you have come to know their meaning and reference. And it is said: al-Ḥakam is the one whose knowledge, speech, and action are correct, so it goes back to these attributes. Al-‘Adl (the Just): what He does is not ugly, so it is a negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya). Al-Laṭīf (the Subtle): the Creator of subtle kindness (luṭf), He is kind to His servants from where they do not know nor expect. And it is said: the Knower of hidden things. On the first, it goes back to act; on the second, to knowledge. Al-Khabīr (the All-Aware): its meaning is al-‘Alīm (the All-Knowing), so it is a knowledge attribute (ṣifa ‘ilmiyya). And it is said: al-Mukhayyir (the One who gives choice), so it is a speech attribute (ṣifa kalāmiyya). Al-Ḥalīm (the Forbearing): He does not hasten punishment for sinners before its appointed time, so it goes back to negation (salb). Al-‘Aẓīm (the Magnificent): its meaning has already passed in the interpretation of al-Jabbār. Al-Ghafūr (the Very Forgiving) is like al-Ghaffār (the Oft-Forgiving) with no difference, on the pattern of al-Raḥmān (the Most Merciful) and al-Raḥīm (the Most Compassionate). Al-Shakūr (the Appreciative): the One who rewards for gratitude, for the recompense of a thing is named after it. And it is said: its meaning is that He establishes abundant grace for little obedience, and on both interpretations it is an active attribute. And it is said: its meaning is the One who praises those who obey Him, so it becomes a speech attribute. Al-‘Alī (the Most High) and al-Kabīr (the All-Great): both are like al-Mutakabbir in meaning. Al-Ḥafīẓ (the Preserver): its meaning is al-‘Alīm (the All-Knowing) as opposed to forgetfulness and heedlessness, and its reference is knowledge. And it is said: the One whom nothing distracts from anything, so its reference is a negative attribute. And it is said: He preserves the forms of things, so it is an active attribute from preservation (ḥifẓ) which opposes loss. Al-Muqīt (the Sustainer): the Creator of provisions, and it is said: the Determiner (al-muqaddir), so on both interpretations it goes back to act. And it is said: its meaning is al-Shahīd (the Witness): the Knower of the absent and present, as will come in its interpretation. Al-Ḥifẓ (preservation).
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and theological commentary on a large number of divine names, proceeding alphabetically or by grouping. For each name, the commentator gives its linguistic derivation, one or more interpretations, and then classifies the resulting attribute as negative (salbiyya), active (fi‘liyya), real (ḥaqīqiyya), speech (kalāmiyya), knowledge (‘ilmiyya), or a combination. The passage begins with al-Ghaffār (Oft-Forgiving), explaining it as an attribute of will (irāda) derived from 'concealment'. Next, al-Qahhār (Subduer) is a negative-active attribute. Al-Wahhāb (Bestower) and al-Razzāq (Provider) are active attributes. Al-Fattāḥ (Opener/Judge) receives multiple interpretations: as 'making easy' or 'creating victory' (active), or as 'the Judge' which can be a speech attribute (if by command) or an attribute of power and will (if by decree). The possibility of 'the Judge' meaning 'the Preventer' is also given, which is active. Al-‘Alīm (All-Knowing) is a real attribute (of essence). Then a series of active attributes: al-Qābiḍ (Constrictor), al-Bāsiṭ (Expander), al-Khāfiḍ (Abaser), al-Rāfi‘ (Exalter), al-Mu‘izz (Honorer), al-Mudhill (Humiliator). The names al-Samī‘ and al-Baṣīr are noted as apparent from earlier discussion. For al-Ḥakam and al-Ḥākim, he reiterates their reference. He mentions a view that al-Ḥakam is the one whose knowledge, speech, and action are correct, spanning multiple attributes. Al-‘Adl (Just) is negative (no ugliness in His actions). Al-Laṭīf (Subtle) is either active (creating kindness) or knowledge (knowing hidden things). Al-Khabīr (All-Aware) is knowledge, but also considered speech (if meaning 'the One who gives choice'). Al-Ḥalīm (Forbearing) is negative (not hastening punishment). Al-‘Aẓīm is referred back to the earlier discussion of al-Jabbār. Al-Ghafūr is synonymous with al-Ghaffār. Al-Shakūr (Appreciative) is active (rewarding gratitude) or speech (praising obedience). Al-‘Alī and al-Kabīr are like al-Mutakabbir. Al-Ḥafīẓ (Preserver) is knowledge (with negation of forgetfulness), or negative (being undisturbed), or active (preserving forms). Al-Muqīt (Sustainer) is active (creating provisions or determining). Finally, al-Shahīd (Witness) is mentioned briefly as knowledge of the absent and present, with a note that it will come later. The passage closes with the word 'al-Ḥifẓ', which may be a marginal note or the start of the next section. Throughout, the commentator shows how the same name can yield different attributive classifications depending on the interpretation, reflecting the richness of the divine names.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Each divine name has only one fixed attribute type.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Fattāḥ has multiple valid interpretations leading to different attribute types (active, speech, power/will).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the name al-Qahhār is classified as which type of attribute?
A) Speech attribute (ṣifa kalāmiyya)
B) Real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya)
C) Negative-active attribute (ṣifa fi‘liyya salbiyya)
D) Active attribute (ṣifa fi‘liyya)

Key Takeaway
The same divine name can be interpreted to yield different attribute types depending on which aspect of its meaning is emphasized.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His name al-Ghaffār, do you truly believe that He wills to remove punishment from you, even if you feel you deserve it? How does this affect your hope in His forgiveness today?
?
The name al-Qahhār reminds us that He is dominant over everything. Is there any area of your life where you are still trying to assert your own dominance, refusing to submit to His absolute control? What would it look like to surrender that area to Him today?
?
Al-Fattāḥ opens doors and makes the difficult easy. Think of one seemingly impossible situation you are facing right now. Will you ask Him to be your Judge and Opener, trusting that His judgment (whether by decree or by command) is perfectly just and good? What step can you take today to rely on His opening?

Divine Attributes
beginner
7 min
Real and Active Attributes: Al-‘Alīm to Al-Mudhill
Al-Jurjani explains al-‘Alīm as a real attribute and al-Qābiḍ through al-Mudhill as active attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real attributes (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya) and active attributes (ṣifa fi‘liyya) using the examples of al-‘Alīm, al-Qābiḍ, al-Bāsiṭ, al-Khāfiḍ, al-Rāfi‘, al-Mu‘izz, and al-Mudhill.

Core Idea
Some attributes belong to God's essence, others describe His actions in relation to creation.

After explaining the first names, the commentator turns to al-‘Alīm, which is a real attribute of essence—meaning it is intrinsic to God. Then a series of names are given that are all active, describing God's actions such as constricting, expanding, abasing, exalting, honoring, and humiliating.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage and translation. Note that al-‘Alīm is treated separately as a real attribute, while the following six names are grouped together as active attributes.
2
Compare real vs. active
Compare al-‘Alīm (real) with al-Qābiḍ (active). A real attribute is part of God's essence; an active attribute involves relating to creation.
Al-‘Alīm means 'Knower of all known things'—a permanent state. The others describe specific actions.
3
Identify the actions
For each active name, identify the action it describes: constricting (qabḍ), expanding (bast), repelling affliction (khafḍ), giving stations (raf‘), giving honor/strength (‘izz), and humiliating (idhlāl).
4
Reflect on the classification
Why is al-‘Alīm called 'real' while the others are 'active'? Consider that real attributes are unchangeable, while active attributes arise from God's will and relation to events.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and theological commentary on a large number of divine names, proceeding alphabetically or by grouping. For each name, the commentator gives its linguistic derivation, one or more interpretations, and then classifies the resulting attribute as negative (salbiyya), active (fi‘liyya), real (ḥaqīqiyya), speech (kalāmiyya), knowledge (‘ilmiyya), or a combination. The passage begins with al-Ghaffār (Oft-Forgiving), explaining it as an attribute of will (irāda) derived from 'concealment'. Next, al-Qahhār (Subduer) is a negative-active attribute. Al-Wahhāb (Bestower) and al-Razzāq (Provider) are active attributes. Al-Fattāḥ (Opener/Judge) receives multiple interpretations: as 'making easy' or 'creating victory' (active), or as 'the Judge' which can be a speech attribute (if by command) or an attribute of power and will (if by decree). The possibility of 'the Judge' meaning 'the Preventer' is also given, which is active. Al-‘Alīm (All-Knowing) is a real attribute (of essence). Then a series of active attributes: al-Qābiḍ (Constrictor), al-Bāsiṭ (Expander), al-Khāfiḍ (Abaser), al-Rāfi‘ (Exalter), al-Mu‘izz (Honorer), al-Mudhill (Humiliator). The names al-Samī‘ and al-Baṣīr are noted as apparent from earlier discussion. For al-Ḥakam and al-Ḥākim, he reiterates their reference. He mentions a view that al-Ḥakam is the one whose knowledge, speech, and action are correct, spanning multiple attributes. Al-‘Adl (Just) is negative (no ugliness in His actions). Al-Laṭīf (Subtle) is either active (creating kindness) or knowledge (knowing hidden things). Al-Khabīr (All-Aware) is knowledge, but also considered speech (if meaning 'the One who gives choice'). Al-Ḥalīm (Forbearing) is negative (not hastening punishment). Al-‘Aẓīm is referred back to the earlier discussion of al-Jabbār. Al-Ghafūr is synonymous with al-Ghaffār. Al-Shakūr (Appreciative) is active (rewarding gratitude) or speech (praising obedience). Al-‘Alī and al-Kabīr are like al-Mutakabbir. Al-Ḥafīẓ (Preserver) is knowledge (with negation of forgetfulness), or negative (being undisturbed), or active (preserving forms). Al-Muqīt (Sustainer) is active (creating provisions or determining). Finally, al-Shahīd (Witness) is mentioned briefly as knowledge of the absent and present, with a note that it will come later. The passage closes with the word 'al-Ḥifẓ', which may be a marginal note or the start of the next section. Throughout, the commentator shows how the same name can yield different attributive classifications depending on the interpretation, reflecting the richness of the divine names.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All attributes describe God's essence equally.
Correction: Some attributes are real and essential (like knowledge), while others are active and relational (like giving).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the name al-‘Alīm is classified as which type of attribute?
A) Real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya)
B) Active attribute (ṣifa fi‘liyya)
C) Negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya)
D) Speech attribute (ṣifa kalāmiyya)

Key Takeaway
Real attributes describe God's essence, while active attributes describe His actions in relation to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you feel 'constricted'—difficulty, loss, or hardship—do you immediately recognize it as an act of al-Qābiḍ, or do you react with complaint? How can you train your heart to see His wisdom in the constriction?
?
Al-‘Alīm is a real attribute: God knows everything about you, including your hidden thoughts and intentions. Does this truth make you feel exposed or comforted? How will it change what you allow to pass through your mind today?
?
If God is the One who humiliates (al-Mudhill) and the One who honors (al-Mu‘izz), then your status in this world is entirely in His hands. Are you more attached to the opinion of people who can honor or humiliate you, or are you seeking honor from the One who truly gives it? What would it look like for you to live with that reality starting now?

Divine Attributes
intermediate

Polysemy in Divine Names: Multiple Classifications
Al-Jurjani explains the multiple interpretations and attribute classifications for eight divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single divine name can have multiple interpretations, each yielding a different attribute classification, as demonstrated by al-Ḥakam, al-‘Adl, al-Laṭīf, al-Khabīr, al-Ḥalīm, al-‘Aẓīm, al-Ghafūr, and al-Shakūr.

Core Idea
Divine names are rich in meaning; their attribute type depends on which aspect is emphasized.

In this section, the commentator shows that many names have multiple valid interpretations, each leading to a different attribute type. This reveals the depth of theological reflection on the names and the importance of linguistic analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage. Notice that for each name, the commentator often gives two or more interpretations, each with a different attribute classification.
2
Identify multiple interpretations
For each name, list the interpretations given. For example, al-Laṭīf can mean 'Creator of subtle kindness' (active) or 'Knower of hidden things' (knowledge).
Note how the same name can be fi‘liyya, ‘ilmiyya, or kalāmiyya.
3
Analyze the classification criteria
Ask: why does one interpretation lead to one type and another to a different type? The classification follows from the aspect of meaning: if the name implies an action, it's active; if it implies speech, it's kalāmiyya; if it implies a negation, it's salbiyya.
4
Reflect on theological diversity
Consider that this polysemy is not a flaw but reflects the richness of the divine names. Multiple interpretations are accepted as long as they are linguistically and theologically sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغفار: أي المريد لإزالة العقوبة عن مستحقها، فهو راجع إلى صفة الإرادة واشتقاقه من الغفر بمعنى الستر. القهار: غالب لا يغلب، فهو صفة فعلية سلبية. الوهاب: كثير العطاء بلا عوض، فيكون صفة فعلية. الرزاق: يرزق من يشاء من الحيوان ما ينتفع به من مأكول ومشروب وملبوس، فهو من الفعل. الفتاح: ميسر العسير، وقيل: خالق الفتح أي النصر، وهو على التقديرين راجع إلى الصفات الفعلية. وقيل: الحاكم، وهو أي الحكم إما بالإخبار والقول فيكون صفة كلامية، أو بالقضاء والقدر فيرجع إلى صفة القدرة والإرادة. والفتاح بمعنى الحاكم مشتق من الفتاحة وهي الحكم. ومنه قوله تعالى: {ربنا افتح بيننا وبين قومنا بالحق} [الأعراف: 89] أي احكم. وقيل: الحاكم معناه المانع، ومنه حكمة اللجام وهي الحديدة المانعة من جماح الدابة، فهو صفة فعلية. العليم: العالم بالمعلومات، فهو صفة حقيقية. القابض: المختص بالسلب، الباسط: المختص بالتوسعة في العطية. الخافض: دافع البلية من الخفض وهو الحط والوضع، الرافع: المعطي للمنازل، المعز: معطي العزة وفي أكثر نسخ الكتاب معطي القوة وكلاهما ظاهر. المذل: الموجب لحط المنزلة، فهذه كلها صفة فعلية. السميع البصير ظاهر معناهما مما سبق. الحكم الحاكم وقد عرفت معناه ومرجعه. وقيل الحكم هو الصحيح علمه وقوله وفعله فيرجع إلى هذه الصفات. العدل: لا يقبح منه ما يفعل، فهو صفة سلبية. اللطيف: خالق اللطف يلطف بعباده من حيث لا يعلمون ولا يحتسبون. وقيل: العالم بالخفيات، فعلى الأول يرجع إلى الفعل وعلى الثاني إلى العلم. الخبير: معناه العليم، فصفة علمية. وقيل: المخير، فصفة كلامية. الحليم: لا يعجل العقاب للعصاة قبل وقته المقدر فيرجع إلى السلب. العظيم قد مر معناه في تفسير الجبار. الغفور كالغفار بلا فرق على قياس الرحمن الرحيم. الشكور: المجازي على الشكر فإن جزاء الشيء يسمى باسمه. وقيل: معناه أنه يثبت على القليل من الطاعة الكثير من النعمة، وعلى التقديرين هو صفة فعلية. وقيل: معناه المثني على من أطاعه فيكون صفة كلامية. العلي الكبير: هما كالمتكبر في المعنى. الحفيظ: معناه العليم من الذي هو ضد السهو والنسيان ومرجعه العلم. وقيل: من لا يشغله شيء عن شيء فمرجعه صفة سلبية. وقيل: يبقي صور الأشياء فصفة فعلية من الحفظ الذي يضاد التضييع. المقيت: خالق الأقوات وقيل: المقدر فيرجع على التقديرين إلى الفعل. وقيل: معناه الشهيد: هو العالم بالغائب والحاضر كما سيأتي في تفسيره.
الحفظ
Al-Ghaffār (the Oft-Forgiving): i.e., the One who wills the removal of punishment from those who deserve it, so it goes back to the attribute of will (irāda), and its derivation is from ghafr meaning concealment. Al-Qahhār (the Subduer): Dominant, not dominated, so it is a negative-active attribute (ṣifa fi‘liyya salbiyya). Al-Wahhāb (the Bestower): Much giving without compensation, so it becomes an active attribute (ṣifa fi‘liyya). Al-Razzāq (the Provider): He provides whomever He wills from living beings with what they benefit from of food, drink, and clothing; thus it is from act. Al-Fattāḥ (the Opener, the Judge): The One who makes the difficult easy, and it is said: the Creator of conquest (fatḥ) i.e., victory, and on both interpretations it goes back to the active attributes. And it is said: al-Ḥākim (the Judge), and it – i.e., judgment (ḥukm) – is either by informing and speech, so it becomes a speech attribute (ṣifa kalāmiyya), or by decree and destiny (qaḍā’ and qadar), so it goes back to the attribute of power and will. And al-Fattāḥ in the meaning of al-Ḥākim is derived from futāḥa which is judgment. And from this is His saying: {Our Lord, judge between us and our people with truth} [al-A‘rāf: 89], i.e., judge. And it is said: al-Ḥākim means the preventing (al-māni‘), and from this is the bit of the bridle (ḥikmat al-lijām) which is the iron that prevents the animal from bolting; thus it is an active attribute. Al-‘Alīm (the All-Knowing): the Knower of all known things, so it is a real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya). Al-Qābiḍ (the Constrictor): the One specifically characterized by withdrawal (salb), Al-Bāsiṭ (the Expander): the One specifically characterized by expansion (tawsi‘a) in giving. Al-Khāfiḍ (the Abaser): the Repeller of affliction, from khafḍ meaning lowering and placing; Al-Rāfi‘ (the Exalter): the Giver of stations; Al-Mu‘izz (the Bestower of honor): the Giver of ‘izza (honor), and in most copies of the book 'the Giver of strength' – both are apparent. Al-Mudhill (the Humiliator): the One who necessitates the lowering of station – all these are active attributes. Al-Samī‘ (the All-Hearing) and al-Baṣīr (the All-Seeing): their meanings are apparent from what preceded. Al-Ḥakam (the Judge) and al-Ḥākim (the Ruler) – you have come to know their meaning and reference. And it is said: al-Ḥakam is the one whose knowledge, speech, and action are correct, so it goes back to these attributes. Al-‘Adl (the Just): what He does is not ugly, so it is a negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya). Al-Laṭīf (the Subtle): the Creator of subtle kindness (luṭf), He is kind to His servants from where they do not know nor expect. And it is said: the Knower of hidden things. On the first, it goes back to act; on the second, to knowledge. Al-Khabīr (the All-Aware): its meaning is al-‘Alīm (the All-Knowing), so it is a knowledge attribute (ṣifa ‘ilmiyya). And it is said: al-Mukhayyir (the One who gives choice), so it is a speech attribute (ṣifa kalāmiyya). Al-Ḥalīm (the Forbearing): He does not hasten punishment for sinners before its appointed time, so it goes back to negation (salb). Al-‘Aẓīm (the Magnificent): its meaning has already passed in the interpretation of al-Jabbār. Al-Ghafūr (the Very Forgiving) is like al-Ghaffār (the Oft-Forgiving) with no difference, on the pattern of al-Raḥmān (the Most Merciful) and al-Raḥīm (the Most Compassionate). Al-Shakūr (the Appreciative): the One who rewards for gratitude, for the recompense of a thing is named after it. And it is said: its meaning is that He establishes abundant grace for little obedience, and on both interpretations it is an active attribute. And it is said: its meaning is the One who praises those who obey Him, so it becomes a speech attribute. Al-‘Alī (the Most High) and al-Kabīr (the All-Great): both are like al-Mutakabbir in meaning. Al-Ḥafīẓ (the Preserver): its meaning is al-‘Alīm (the All-Knowing) as opposed to forgetfulness and heedlessness, and its reference is knowledge. And it is said: the One whom nothing distracts from anything, so its reference is a negative attribute. And it is said: He preserves the forms of things, so it is an active attribute from preservation (ḥifẓ) which opposes loss. Al-Muqīt (the Sustainer): the Creator of provisions, and it is said: the Determiner (al-muqaddir), so on both interpretations it goes back to act. And it is said: its meaning is al-Shahīd (the Witness): the Knower of the absent and present, as will come in its interpretation. Al-Ḥifẓ (preservation).
Source pages p. 237

Commentary

For each name, the commentator gives its linguistic derivation, one or more interpretations, and then classifies the resulting attribute as negative (salbiyya), active (fi‘liyya), real (ḥaqīqiyya), speech (kalāmiyya), knowledge (‘ilmiyya), or a combination. According to the passage, the name al-‘Adl is classified as which type of attribute?
A) Active attribute (ṣifa fi‘liyya)

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-Shakūr, do you restrict His appreciation to merely rewarding you, or do you also consider that He praises your obedience? How does the awareness of this deeper meaning change the way you seek His pleasure? This week, before supplication, take a moment to reflect on a different interpretation of a divine name you regularly use.
?
The name al-Ḥalīm tells us that Allah does not hasten punishment. Have you ever taken advantage of His forbearance, delaying repentance? Let this recognition stir you to turn back to Him sincerely, without delay, knowing that His patience is meant to lead you to repentance, not to continue in sin.
?
The passage shows that scholars debated the classification of divine names. Do you approach the names of Allah with the same depth of reflection, or do you settle for superficial meanings? Commit to studying one divine name this week with the aim of exploring its multiple dimensions and how it impacts your relationship with Allah.

Remaining Names: Al-‘Alī, Al-Kabīr, Al-Ḥafīẓ, Al-Muqīt, Al-Shahīd
Al-Jurjani explains the final five names and their attribute classifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize the different attribute classifications for the divine names al-‘Alī, al-Kabīr, al-Ḥafīẓ, al-Muqīt, and al-Shahīd, and understand that even seemingly straightforward names can have multiple interpretations.

Core Idea
Even names that appear straightforward can have multiple interpretations and attribute types.

The passage concludes with a few more names, each again showing the pattern of interpretation and classification. The final word 'al-Ḥifẓ' may indicate the beginning of the next section, but the lesson focuses on the names presented.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the final part of the passage. Note that al-‘Alī and al-Kabīr are grouped together, and each of al-Ḥafīẓ, al-Muqīt, and al-Shahīd has multiple interpretations.
2
Identify the classifications
For each name, determine the attribute type(s) given. For example, al-Ḥafīẓ has three possible classifications: knowledge, negative, or active.
Notice how the same name can belong to different categories.
3
Compare with earlier names
Compare these names with those in previous lessons. What patterns do you see? For instance, many names have a knowledge interpretation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with al-Shahīd and a note that it will be explained later. This indicates that the commentary is part of a larger work.

Al-Fattāḥ (the Opener, the Judge): The One who makes the difficult easy, and it is said: the Creator of conquest (fatḥ) i.e., victory, and on both interpretations it goes back to the active attributes. And it is said: al-Ḥākim (the Judge), and it – i.e., judgment (ḥukm) – is either by informing and speech, so it becomes a speech attribute (ṣifa kalāmiyya), or by decree and destiny (qaḍā’ and qadar), so it goes back to the attribute of power and will. And al-Fattāḥ in the meaning of al-Ḥākim is derived from futāḥa which is judgment. And from this is His saying: {Our Lord, judge between us and our people with truth} [al-A‘rāf: 89], i.e., judge. And it is said: al-Ḥākim means the preventing (al-māni‘), and from this is the bit of the bridle (ḥikmat al-lijām) which is the iron that prevents the animal from bolting; thus it is an active attribute. Al-‘Alīm (the All-Knowing): the Knower of all known things, so it is a real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya). Al-Qābiḍ (the Constrictor): the One specifically characterized by withdrawal (salb), Al-Bāsiṭ (the Expander): the One specifically characterized by expansion (tawsi‘a) in giving. Al-Khāfiḍ (the Abaser): the Repeller of affliction, from khafḍ meaning lowering and placing; Al-Rāfi‘ (the Exalter): the Giver of stations; Al-Mu‘izz (the Bestower of honor): the Giver of ‘izza (honor), and in most copies of the book 'the Giver of strength' – both are apparent. Al-Mudhill (the Humiliator): the One who necessitates the lowering of station – all these are active attributes. Al-Samī‘ (the All-Hearing) and al-Baṣīr (the All-Seeing): their meanings are apparent from what preceded. Al-Ḥakam (the Judge) and al-Ḥākim (the Ruler) – you have come to know their meaning and reference. And it is said: al-Ḥakam is the one whose knowledge, speech, and action are correct, so it goes back to these attributes. Al-‘Adl (the Just): what He does is not ugly, so it is a negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya). Al-Laṭīf (the Subtle): the Creator of subtle kindness (luṭf), He is kind to His servants from where they do not know nor expect. And it is said: the Knower of hidden things. On the first, it goes back to act; on the second, to knowledge. Al-Khabīr (the All-Aware): its meaning is al-‘Alīm (the All-Knowing), so it is a knowledge attribute (ṣifa ‘ilmiyya). And it is said: al-Mukhayyir (the One who gives choice), so it is a speech attribute (ṣifa kalāmiyya). Al-Ḥalīm (the Forbearing): He does not hasten punishment for sinners before its appointed time, so it goes back to negation (salb). Al-‘Aẓīm (the Magnificent): its meaning has already passed in the interpretation of al-Jabbār. Al-Ghafūr (the Very Forgiving) is like al-Ghaffār (the Oft-Forgiving) with no difference, on the pattern of al-Raḥmān (the Most Merciful) and al-Raḥīm (the Most Compassionate). Al-Shakūr (the Appreciative): the One who rewards for gratitude, for the recompense of a thing is named after it. And it is said: its meaning is that He establishes abundant grace for little obedience, and on both interpretations it is an active attribute. And it is said: its meaning is the One who praises those who obey Him, so it becomes a speech attribute. Al-‘Alī (the Most High) and al-Kabīr (the All-Great): both are like al-Mutakabbir in meaning. Al-Ḥafīẓ (the Preserver): its meaning is al-‘Alīm (the All-Knowing) as opposed to forgetfulness and heedlessness, and its reference is knowledge. And it is said: the One whom nothing distracts from anything, so its reference is a negative attribute. And it is said: He preserves the forms of things, so it is an active attribute from preservation (ḥifẓ) which opposes loss. Al-Muqīt (the Sustainer): the Creator of provisions, and it is said: the Determiner (al-muqaddir), so on both interpretations it goes back to act. And it is said: its meaning is al-Shahīd (the Witness): the Knower of the absent and present, as will come in its interpretation. Al-Ḥifẓ (preservation).
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and theological commentary on a large number of divine names, proceeding alphabetically or by grouping. For each name, the commentator gives its linguistic derivation, one or more interpretations, and then classifies the resulting attribute as negative (salbiyya), active (fi‘liyya), real (ḥaqīqiyya), speech (kalāmiyya), knowledge (‘ilmiyya), or a combination. The passage begins with al-Ghaffār (Oft-Forgiving), explaining it as an attribute of will (irāda) derived from 'concealment'. Next, al-Qahhār (Subduer) is a negative-active attribute. Al-Wahhāb (Bestower) and al-Razzāq (Provider) are active attributes. Al-Fattāḥ (Opener/Judge) receives multiple interpretations: as 'making easy' or 'creating victory' (active), or as 'the Judge' which can be a speech attribute (if by command) or an attribute of power and will (if by decree). The possibility of 'the Judge' meaning 'the Preventer' is also given, which is active. Al-‘Alīm (All-Knowing) is a real attribute (of essence). Then a series of active attributes: al-Qābiḍ (Constrictor), al-Bāsiṭ (Expander), al-Khāfiḍ (Abaser), al-Rāfi‘ (Exalter), al-Mu‘izz (Honorer), al-Mudhill (Humiliator). The names al-Samī‘ and al-Baṣīr are noted as apparent from earlier discussion. For al-Ḥakam and al-Ḥākim, he reiterates their reference. He mentions a view that al-Ḥakam is the one whose knowledge, speech, and action are correct, spanning multiple attributes. Al-‘Adl (Just) is negative (no ugliness in His actions). Al-Laṭīf (Subtle) is either active (creating kindness) or knowledge (knowing hidden things). Al-Khabīr (All-Aware) is knowledge, but also considered speech (if meaning 'the One who gives choice'). Al-Ḥalīm (Forbearing) is negative (not hastening punishment). Al-‘Aẓīm is referred back to the earlier discussion of al-Jabbār. Al-Ghafūr is synonymous with al-Ghaffār. Al-Shakūr (Appreciative) is active (rewarding gratitude) or speech (praising obedience). Al-‘Alī and al-Kabīr are like al-Mutakabbir. Al-Ḥafīẓ (Preserver) is knowledge (with negation of forgetfulness), or negative (being undisturbed), or active (preserving forms). Al-Muqīt (Sustainer) is active (creating provisions or determining). Finally, al-Shahīd (Witness) is mentioned briefly as knowledge of the absent and present, with a note that it will come later. The passage closes with the word 'al-Ḥifẓ', which may be a marginal note or the start of the next section. Throughout, the commentator shows how the same name can yield different attributive classifications depending on the interpretation, reflecting the richness of the divine names.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-‘Alī and al-Kabīr are only about height and size metaphorically.
Correction: They are interpreted as attributes of magnificence, similar to al-Mutakabbir, implying divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the name al-Muqīt is classified as which type of attribute?
A) Knowledge attribute (ṣifa ‘ilmiyya)
B) Active attribute (ṣifa fi‘liyya)
C) Negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya)
D) Speech attribute (ṣifa kalāmiyya)

Key Takeaway
Even names that seem straightforward can have multiple theological interpretations and attribute classifications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that al-Ḥafīẓ includes the meaning that nothing distracts Him. When you are distracted in prayer or forget God in your busy day, how does that reality expose your own need to cultivate this attribute in your awareness? What specific step will you take this week to act ‘undistracted’ in your devotion?
?
If al-Muqīt is both the Creator and Determiner of your provisions, then any anxiety about your career, income, or future becomes a failure to trust this name. What is the one area of provision you still struggle to leave in His hands, and what would it cost you to surrender it fully today?
?
The name al-Shahīd means He knows the absent and present – nothing is hidden from Him. Knowing that He sees even your secret thoughts and motives, what is the one thing you have been hiding from others (or even from yourself) that you now realize must be brought into the light of repentance?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Meanings of Divine Names: From Al-Ghaffar to Al-Mudhill
Commentary on nine divine names from al-Ghaffār to al-Mudhill.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings and attribute categories of the first set of divine names, including active, negative, real, and verbal attributes.

Core Idea
Each divine name has a specific meaning and corresponds to a distinct attribute category.

The commentator begins by explaining the names al-Ghaffar through al-Mudhill, showing how each name's meaning determines its classification among the divine attributes. This lesson introduces the method of categorizing names based on their semantic content and linguistic derivation.

Guided Path
1
Read the names and definitions
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of the names and their meanings. Then read the literal translation to understand each definition.
Notice that each name is followed by a brief interpretation and a category assignment.
2
Identify attribute categories
For each name, identify whether it is classified as a real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya), active attribute (ṣifa fiʿliyya), negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya), or verbal attribute (ṣifa kalāmiyya).
Some names may have multiple interpretations leading to different categories.
3
Reflect on linguistic derivations
Pay attention to how the commentator uses Arabic roots and Quranic verses to justify the meaning and category of each name.
For example, al-Ghaffār is derived from 'ghafr' meaning concealment, linking it to the will.

( المجيب يجيب الأدعية . الواسع هو الذي وسع جوده جميع وعلمه جميع المعلومات وقدرته جميع المقدورات فلا يشغله شأن عن شأن. ( الحكيم العليم ذو اللحمة وهي العلم بالأشياء على ما هي عليه والإتيان بالأفعال على ما ينبغي، وقيل: الحكيم بمعنى المحكم من الأحكام وهو إتقان التدبير وإحسان التقدير ( الودود المودود) من الود وهو المحبة ) كالحلوب والركوب ) بمعنى المحلوب والمركوب ( وقيل : ( معناه الواد كالصبور بمعنى الصابر) أي يود ثناءه على المطيع وثوابه له المجيد الجميل أفعاله وقيل : الكثير أفضاله ، وقيل : لا يشارك فيما له من أوصاف المدح الباعث المعيد للخلائق يوم القيامة ( الشهيد العالم بالغائب والحاضر الحق) معناه ( العدل وقيل : الواجب لذاته أي لا يفتقر في وجوده إلى غيره . ( وقيل : ( معناه ( المحق أي الصادق ( في القول ( وقيل : مظهر الحق الوكيل المتكفل بأمور الخلق وحاجاتهم. ( وقيل : الموكول إِليه ذلك ) فإن عباده وكلوا إليه مصالحهم اعتماداً على إحسانه ( القوي، القادر على كل أمر، المتين) . قال الآمدي : معناه نفي النهاية في القدرة يعني أن قدرته لا تتناهى وفي عبارة الكتاب ( هي النهاية في القدرة ولا يبعد أن يكون تصحيفاً والأظهر أن يراد أن المتانة هي بلوغ القدرة ) إلى النهاية والغاية وذلك إذا كانت غير متناهية الولي : الحافظ للولاية ) أي النصرة فمعناه الناصر . ( وقيل : هو بمعنى المتولي للأمر والقائم به الحميد المحمود فهو قوله : (وقيل: هو المتصف بصفات الجلال والإكرام :قيل ينبغي أن يحمل صفة الجلال والجمال على صفة القهر واللطف حتى يكون مغايراً لمعنى المتكبر وإلا فإن أريد بهما الصفة السلبية والثبوتية كما قيل في تفسيرها لم يكن لقول الشارح وقيل إلخ فائدة، فتأمل .
Al-Ghaffar (the Oft-Forgiving): meaning the One who intends to remove punishment from its deserving ones, so it refers to the attribute of will, and its derivation is from 'ghafr' meaning concealment. Al-Qahhar (the Subduer): dominant, not dominated, so it is an active-negative attribute. Al-Wahhab (the Bestower): abundant giving without compensation, thus an active attribute. Ar-Razzaq (the Provider): provides for whom He wills of living beings with what benefits them from food, drink, and clothing, so it is from action. Al-Fattah (the Opener): the one who eases difficulty, and it is said: the creator of victory (i.e., triumph), and in both cases it refers to active attributes. And it is said: Al-Hakam (the Judge), and judgment is either by informing and speech, so it is a verbal attribute, or by decree and predestination, so it refers to the attributes of power and will. Al-Fattah in the sense of judge is derived from 'fattaha' which is judgment, and from it is His saying: 'Our Lord, judge between us and our people with truth' [Al-A'raf:89], i.e., judge. And it is said: Al-Hakam means the restrainer, and from it is 'hukmat al-lijam' (the bit) which is the iron piece that prevents the animal from bolting, so it is an active attribute. Al-'Alim (the All-Knowing): the knower of all known things, so it is a real attribute. Al-Qabid (the Constrictor): the one who withholds, Al-Basit (the Expander): the one who expands in giving. Al-Khafid (the Abaser): the repeller of affliction, from 'khafd' meaning lowering and placement. Ar-Rafi' (the Exalter): the giver of high stations. Al-Mu'izz (the Honorer): the giver of honor, and in most copies of the book 'the giver of strength', both are clear. Al-Mudhill (the Humiliator): the one who causes lowering of status. All these are active attributes. As-Sami' (the All-Hearing) and Al-Basir (the All-Seeing): their meanings are apparent from what preceded. Al-Hakam (the Judge): you have known its meaning and reference. And it is said: Al-Hakam is the one whose knowledge, speech, and action are correct, so it refers to these attributes. Al-'Adl (the Just): nothing disgraceful proceeds from Him, so it is a negative attribute. Al-Latif (the Subtle): the creator of subtle goodness, He is kind to His servants in ways they do not know or expect. And it is said: the knower of hidden things. On the first view it refers to action, on the second to knowledge. Al-Khabir (the All-Aware): its meaning is Al-'Alim, so it is an attribute of knowledge. And it is said: the one who gives good tidings, so it is a verbal attribute. Al-Halim (the Forbearing): does not hasten punishment to sinners before its appointed time, so it refers to negation. Al-'Azim (the Magnificent): its meaning has passed in the explanation of Al-Jabbar. Al-Ghafur (the Forgiving) is like Al-Ghaffar with no difference, on the analogy of Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim. Ash-Shakur (the Appreciative): the one who rewards for gratitude, for the recompense of a thing is named after it. And it is said: its meaning is that He gives much reward for a little obedience, and in both cases it is an active attribute. And it is said: its meaning is the one who praises those who obey Him, so it would be a verbal attribute. Al-'Ali (the Most High) and Al-Kabir (the Great): both are like Al-Mutakabbir in meaning. Al-Hafiz (the Preserver): its meaning is Al-'Alim, from that which is opposite of forgetfulness and negligence, and its reference is knowledge. And it is said: the one whom no thing occupies from another thing, so its reference is a negative attribute. And it is said: the one who preserves the forms of things, so it is an active attribute from preservation which is opposite of loss. Al-Muqit (the Nourisher): the creator of provisions, and it is said: the determiner, so in both views it refers to action. And it is said: its meaning is Ash-Shahid (the Witness): He is the knower of the unseen and the present, as will come in its interpretation. Al-Hifz (the Preservation) of beings, so it refers to knowledge. And it is said: Al-Muqtadir (the Powerful) refers to power. Al-Hasib (the Reckoner): the sufficient one who creates what suffices the servants in their interests and needs, so it is an active attribute, from their saying: 'So-and-so honored me and hasabani, i.e., gave me until I said 'hasbi' (enough for me).' And it is said: the one who calls His servants to account for what they have done of good and evil, so it refers to a verbal attribute. Al-Jalil (the Majestic): like Al-Mutakabbir, and it is said: the one described with the attributes of majesty and beauty. Al-Karim (the Generous): the possessor of generosity, and it is said: the one capable of generosity, and their reference is action and power. And it is said: its meaning is the exalted in rank, and from it is 'kara'im al-mawashi' (choice livestock) for their preciousness, so it refers to an attributive relation. And it is said: He forgives sins. Ar-Raqib (the Watchful): like Al-Hafiz. Al-Ghazali said: it is more specific than Al-Hafiz because Ar-Raqib is the one who watches a thing such that he never neglects it at all and observes it with a constant, necessary observation such that if the one forbidden from that thing knew of it, he would not proceed, so it seems to refer to knowledge and preservation, but with regard to necessity and in relation to someone forbidden from a thing guarded from handling. Al-Mujib (the Answerer of prayers): He answers supplications. Al-Wasi' (the Vast): He whose generosity encompasses all, His knowledge all known things, and His power all possible things, so no matter occupies Him from another. Al-Hakim (the Wise): Al-'Alim (the All-Knowing) possessing wisdom, which is knowledge of things as they are and performing actions as they ought. And it is said: Al-Hakim in the sense of Al-Muhkim (the Perfect) from 'ihkam' (perfection), which is precision of management and excellence of estimation. Al-Wadud (the Loving): the one who is loved, from 'wudd' which is love, like 'halub' and 'rakub' meaning the one milked and the one ridden. And it is said: its meaning is Al-Wad (the Lover) like 'sabur' meaning patient, i.e., He loves the praise of the obedient and His reward for him. Al-Majid (the Glorious): the beautiful in His actions, and it is said: the abundant in His bounties, and it is said: He has no partner in His attributes of praise. Al-Ba'ith (the Resurrector): the one who brings back creation on the Day of Resurrection. Ash-Shahid (the Witness): the knower of the unseen and the present. Al-Haqq (the Truth): its meaning is Al-'Adl (the Just), and it is said: the self-necessary, i.e., He does not need another in His existence. And it is said: its meaning is Al-Muhiqq (the Realizer), i.e., the truthful in speech. And it is said: the manifest of truth. Al-Wakil (the Trustee): the one who undertakes the affairs and needs of creation. And it is said: the one to whom that is entrusted, for His servants entrust their interests to Him relying on His beneficence. Al-Qawi (the Strong): the powerful over every matter. Al-Matin (the Firm): Al-Amidi said: its meaning is the negation of limit in power, meaning that His power is infinite. And in the wording of the book: 'It is the ultimate in power,' and it is not unlikely to be a corruption, and the apparent meaning is that 'matanah' is the reaching of power to the ultimate and extreme, which occurs when it is infinite. Al-Wali (the Guardian): the preserver of alliance, i.e., assistance, so its meaning is the Helper. And it is said: it means the one who undertakes the affair and stands by it. Al-Hamid (the Praiseworthy): the praised. [Marginal note]: His saying: 'And it is said: He is the one described with the attributes of majesty and honor' – it is said: the attributes of majesty and beauty should be interpreted as the attributes of subjugation and kindness so that they differ from the meaning of Al-Mutakabbir; otherwise, if they are intended as negative and positive attributes as is said in their interpretation, then the commentator's saying 'and it is said' etc. would have no benefit. So reflect.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a detailed lexical and theological commentary on the divine names (al-asmā' al-ḥusnā). The author proceeds name by name, explaining each name's literal meaning, its derivation, and its categorization among the divine attributes. The overall pattern is as follows: For each name, the author gives a definition (often citing alternative views), then classifies it as either a ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya (real attribute, e.g., knowledge, power), ṣifa fiʿliyya (active attribute, e.g., providing, forgiving), ṣifa salbiyya (negative attribute, e.g., not unjust, not negligent), or ṣifa kalāmiyya (verbal attribute, e.g., judging by speech). Occasionally, he notes when a name can be understood in multiple ways, each leading to a different attribute category. The commentary draws on linguistic analysis and Quranic usage. For example, al-Ghaffār is linked to the will to remove punishment, making it a volitional attribute. Al-Qahhār is active-negative because it implies dominance over others. Al-ʿAlīm is a real attribute because knowledge is inherent. The passage also includes notable opinions from earlier scholars like al-Ghazālī, particularly on the distinction between al-Raqīb and al-Ḥafīẓ. The marginal note at the end discusses a subtle point: how to interpret 'al-Jalāl' and 'al-Jamāl' to avoid redundancy with al-Mutakabbir. The author advises reflecting on the commentator's phrasing. Overall, the passage demonstrates the method of tawqīfī (revelation-based) naming: each name is taken from revelation and explained rationally in terms of its theological import.

الصفة الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the divine names are mere synonyms or arbitrary labels without specific meanings.
Correction: Each name has a precise meaning and corresponds to a distinct divine attribute category, as shown by the commentator's lexical and theological analysis.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, to which category does the name 'al-ʿAlīm' (the All-Knowing) belong?
A) Active attribute (ṣifa fiʿliyya)
B) Negative attribute (ṣifa salbiyya)
C) Real attribute (ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya)
D) Verbal attribute (ṣifa kalāmiyya)

Key Takeaway
The first set of names illustrates the diversity of divine attributes: real (knowledge), active (provision, restraint, etc.), negative (subduing), and verbal (judging by speech).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that Allah is al-Ghaffār, the One who intends to remove punishment. Is there a sin you keep committing, assuming that because you haven't been punished yet, Allah is indifferent? What does it mean that He 'intends' to remove punishment from you? How will you respond to that intention tonight in your prayer?
?
Al-Mudhill causes lowering of status. Think of a person or group you look down on. Could your low view of them be a reflection of pride in yourself? What would it mean if Allah humiliated you for that pride? Will you repent from that judgment before this day ends?
?
You have seen that al-ʿAlīm is a real attribute—Allah knows everything inherently. What secret thought or habit are you hiding, thinking no one sees? How does the reality that Allah knows it change what you should do with it right now?

Meanings of Divine Names: From As-Sami' to Al-Karim
Commentary on divine names from As-Sami' to Al-Karim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings and attribute categories of the intermediate set of divine names, including sensory, verbal, negative, and active attributes.

Core Idea
Divine names like As-Sami' and Al-Basir are self-evident, while others like Al-'Adl and Al-Halim show negative attributes.

The commentator continues with names from As-Sami' and Al-Basir through Al-Karim. Many of these names have multiple interpretations, each leading to a different attribute category. This lesson highlights the importance of understanding context and derivations.

Guided Path
1
Read the names and their definitions
Read the Arabic passage to see the variety of names and the alternative interpretations provided by the commentator.
Some names like As-Sami' are said to be 'apparent' (zāhir) in meaning.
2
Note alternative interpretations
Many names have multiple possible meanings that lead to different categorizations (e.g., Al-Latif can be active or real). Pay attention to the 'qīla' (it is said) phrases.
This shows the depth of theological analysis.
3
Identify the categories
For each name, see if the commentator classifies it as real, active, negative, verbal, or attributive relation.
For example, Al-'Adl is negative, Al-Halim is negative, Ash-Shakur is active or verbal.

( الحكيم العليم ذو اللحمة وهي العلم بالأشياء على ما هي عليه والإتيان بالأفعال على ما ينبغي، وقيل: الحكيم بمعنى المحكم من الأحكام وهو إتقان التدبير وإحسان التقدير ( الودود المودود) من الود وهو المحبة ) كالحلوب والركوب ) بمعنى المحلوب والمركوب ( وقيل : ( معناه الواد كالصبور بمعنى الصابر) أي يود ثناءه على المطيع وثوابه له المجيد الجميل أفعاله وقيل : الكثير أفضاله ، وقيل : لا يشارك فيما له من أوصاف المدح الباعث المعيد للخلائق يوم القيامة ( الشهيد العالم بالغائب والحاضر الحق) معناه ( العدل وقيل : الواجب لذاته أي لا يفتقر في وجوده إلى غيره . ( وقيل : ( معناه ( المحق أي الصادق ( في القول ( وقيل : مظهر الحق الوكيل المتكفل بأمور الخلق وحاجاتهم. ( وقيل : الموكول إِليه ذلك ) فإن عباده وكلوا إليه مصالحهم اعتماداً على إحسانه ( القوي، القادر على كل أمر، المتين) . قال الآمدي : معناه نفي النهاية في القدرة يعني أن قدرته لا تتناهى وفي عبارة الكتاب ( هي النهاية في القدرة ولا يبعد أن يكون تصحيفاً والأظهر أن يراد أن المتانة هي بلوغ القدرة ) إلى النهاية والغاية وذلك إذا كانت غير متناهية الولي : الحافظ للولاية ) أي النصرة فمعناه الناصر . ( وقيل : هو بمعنى المتولي للأمر والقائم به الحميد المحمود فهو قوله : (وقيل: هو المتصف بصفات الجلال والإكرام :قيل ينبغي أن يحمل صفة الجلال والجمال على صفة القهر واللطف حتى يكون مغايراً لمعنى المتكبر وإلا فإن أريد بهما الصفة السلبية والثبوتية كما قيل في تفسيرها لم يكن لقول الشارح وقيل إلخ فائدة، فتأمل .
Al-Ghaffar (the Oft-Forgiving): meaning the One who intends to remove punishment from its deserving ones, so it refers to the attribute of will, and its derivation is from 'ghafr' meaning concealment. Al-Qahhar (the Subduer): dominant, not dominated, so it is an active-negative attribute. Al-Wahhab (the Bestower): abundant giving without compensation, thus an active attribute. Ar-Razzaq (the Provider): provides for whom He wills of living beings with what benefits them from food, drink, and clothing, so it is from action. Al-Fattah (the Opener): the one who eases difficulty, and it is said: the creator of victory (i.e., triumph), and in both cases it refers to active attributes. And it is said: Al-Hakam (the Judge), and judgment is either by informing and speech, so it is a verbal attribute, or by decree and predestination, so it refers to the attributes of power and will. Al-Fattah in the sense of judge is derived from 'fattaha' which is judgment, and from it is His saying: 'Our Lord, judge between us and our people with truth' [Al-A'raf:89], i.e., judge. And it is said: Al-Hakam means the restrainer, and from it is 'hukmat al-lijam' (the bit) which is the iron piece that prevents the animal from bolting, so it is an active attribute. Al-'Alim (the All-Knowing): the knower of all known things, so it is a real attribute. Al-Qabid (the Constrictor): the one who withholds, Al-Basit (the Expander): the one who expands in giving. Al-Khafid (the Abaser): the repeller of affliction, from 'khafd' meaning lowering and placement. Ar-Rafi' (the Exalter): the giver of high stations. Al-Mu'izz (the Honorer): the giver of honor, and in most copies of the book 'the giver of strength', both are clear. Al-Mudhill (the Humiliator): the one who causes lowering of status. All these are active attributes. As-Sami' (the All-Hearing) and Al-Basir (the All-Seeing): their meanings are apparent from what preceded. Al-Hakam (the Judge): you have known its meaning and reference. And it is said: Al-Hakam is the one whose knowledge, speech, and action are correct, so it refers to these attributes. Al-'Adl (the Just): nothing disgraceful proceeds from Him, so it is a negative attribute. Al-Latif (the Subtle): the creator of subtle goodness, He is kind to His servants in ways they do not know or expect. And it is said: the knower of hidden things. On the first view it refers to action, on the second to knowledge. Al-Khabir (the All-Aware): its meaning is Al-'Alim, so it is an attribute of knowledge. And it is said: the one who gives good tidings, so it is a verbal attribute. Al-Halim (the Forbearing): does not hasten punishment to sinners before its appointed time, so it refers to negation. Al-'Azim (the Magnificent): its meaning has passed in the explanation of Al-Jabbar. Al-Ghafur (the Forgiving) is like Al-Ghaffar with no difference, on the analogy of Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim. Ash-Shakur (the Appreciative): the one who rewards for gratitude, for the recompense of a thing is named after it. And it is said: its meaning is that He gives much reward for a little obedience, and in both cases it is an active attribute. And it is said: its meaning is the one who praises those who obey Him, so it would be a verbal attribute. Al-'Ali (the Most High) and Al-Kabir (the Great): both are like Al-Mutakabbir in meaning. Al-Hafiz (the Preserver): its meaning is Al-'Alim, from that which is opposite of forgetfulness and negligence, and its reference is knowledge. And it is said: the one whom no thing occupies from another thing, so its reference is a negative attribute. And it is said: the one who preserves the forms of things, so it is an active attribute from preservation which is opposite of loss. Al-Muqit (the Nourisher): the creator of provisions, and it is said: the determiner, so in both views it refers to action. And it is said: its meaning is Ash-Shahid (the Witness): He is the knower of the unseen and the present, as will come in its interpretation. Al-Hifz (the Preservation) of beings, so it refers to knowledge. And it is said: Al-Muqtadir (the Powerful) refers to power. Al-Hasib (the Reckoner): the sufficient one who creates what suffices the servants in their interests and needs, so it is an active attribute, from their saying: 'So-and-so honored me and hasabani, i.e., gave me until I said 'hasbi' (enough for me).' And it is said: the one who calls His servants to account for what they have done of good and evil, so it refers to a verbal attribute. Al-Jalil (the Majestic): like Al-Mutakabbir, and it is said: the one described with the attributes of majesty and beauty. Al-Karim (the Generous): the possessor of generosity, and it is said: the one capable of generosity, and their reference is action and power. And it is said: its meaning is the exalted in rank, and from it is 'kara'im al-mawashi' (choice livestock) for their preciousness, so it refers to an attributive relation. And it is said: He forgives sins. Ar-Raqib (the Watchful): like Al-Hafiz. Al-Ghazali said: it is more specific than Al-Hafiz because Ar-Raqib is the one who watches a thing such that he never neglects it at all and observes it with a constant, necessary observation such that if the one forbidden from that thing knew of it, he would not proceed, so it seems to refer to knowledge and preservation, but with regard to necessity and in relation to someone forbidden from a thing guarded from handling. Al-Mujib (the Answerer of prayers): He answers supplications. Al-Wasi' (the Vast): He whose generosity encompasses all, His knowledge all known things, and His power all possible things, so no matter occupies Him from another. Al-Hakim (the Wise): Al-'Alim (the All-Knowing) possessing wisdom, which is knowledge of things as they are and performing actions as they ought. And it is said: Al-Hakim in the sense of Al-Muhkim (the Perfect) from 'ihkam' (perfection), which is precision of management and excellence of estimation. Al-Wadud (the Loving): the one who is loved, from 'wudd' which is love, like 'halub' and 'rakub' meaning the one milked and the one ridden. And it is said: its meaning is Al-Wad (the Lover) like 'sabur' meaning patient, i.e., He loves the praise of the obedient and His reward for him. Al-Majid (the Glorious): the beautiful in His actions, and it is said: the abundant in His bounties, and it is said: He has no partner in His attributes of praise. Al-Ba'ith (the Resurrector): the one who brings back creation on the Day of Resurrection. Ash-Shahid (the Witness): the knower of the unseen and the present. Al-Haqq (the Truth): its meaning is Al-'Adl (the Just), and it is said: the self-necessary, i.e., He does not need another in His existence. And it is said: its meaning is Al-Muhiqq (the Realizer), i.e., the truthful in speech. And it is said: the manifest of truth. Al-Wakil (the Trustee): the one who undertakes the affairs and needs of creation. And it is said: the one to whom that is entrusted, for His servants entrust their interests to Him relying on His beneficence. Al-Qawi (the Strong): the powerful over every matter. Al-Matin (the Firm): Al-Amidi said: its meaning is the negation of limit in power, meaning that His power is infinite. And in the wording of the book: 'It is the ultimate in power,' and it is not unlikely to be a corruption, and the apparent meaning is that 'matanah' is the reaching of power to the ultimate and extreme, which occurs when it is infinite. Al-Wali (the Guardian): the preserver of alliance, i.e., assistance, so its meaning is the Helper. And it is said: it means the one who undertakes the affair and stands by it. Al-Hamid (the Praiseworthy): the praised. [Marginal note]: His saying: 'And it is said: He is the one described with the attributes of majesty and honor' – it is said: the attributes of majesty and beauty should be interpreted as the attributes of subjugation and kindness so that they differ from the meaning of Al-Mutakabbir; otherwise, if they are intended as negative and positive attributes as is said in their interpretation, then the commentator's saying 'and it is said' etc. would have no benefit. So reflect.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a detailed lexical and theological commentary on the divine names (al-asmā' al-ḥusnā). The author proceeds name by name, explaining each name's literal meaning, its derivation, and its categorization among the divine attributes. The overall pattern is as follows: For each name, the author gives a definition (often citing alternative views), then classifies it as either a ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya (real attribute, e.g., knowledge, power), ṣifa fiʿliyya (active attribute, e.g., providing, forgiving), ṣifa salbiyya (negative attribute, e.g., not unjust, not negligent), or ṣifa kalāmiyya (verbal attribute, e.g., judging by speech). Occasionally, he notes when a name can be understood in multiple ways, each leading to a different attribute category. The commentary draws on linguistic analysis and Quranic usage. For example, al-Ghaffār is linked to the will to remove punishment, making it a volitional attribute. Al-Qahhār is active-negative because it implies dominance over others. Al-ʿAlīm is a real attribute because knowledge is inherent. The passage also includes notable opinions from earlier scholars like al-Ghazālī, particularly on the distinction between al-Raqīb and al-Ḥafīẓ. The marginal note at the end discusses a subtle point: how to interpret 'al-Jalāl' and 'al-Jamāl' to avoid redundancy with al-Mutakabbir. The author advises reflecting on the commentator's phrasing. Overall, the passage demonstrates the method of tawqīfī (revelation-based) naming: each name is taken from revelation and explained rationally in terms of its theological import.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which category does the name 'Al-'Adl' belong to?
A) Active attribute
B) Negative attribute
C) Real attribute

Key Takeaway
Many divine names have multiple layers of meaning, each leading to a different attribute category, reflecting richness in theological understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider Al-Halim, who does not hasten punishment despite your repeated failings, what specific habit or sin have you been presuming upon His forbearance? How will you repent today knowing His patience is not an invitation to persist?
?
Al-Karim is said to forgive sins and is the possessor of generosity. Are there sins you have hidden from yourself, convinced they are too small or too great for His forgiveness? What one sin will you bring to Al-Karim right now, trusting His generosity?
?
The name Al-Hafiz means He preserves what is precious to Him. Do you truly believe He preserves your faith through trials, or do you rely on your own efforts? What would change in your daily struggle if you acted on the belief that Al-Hafiz is the one who keeps you?

Meanings of Divine Names: From Ar-Raqib to Al-Hamid and Marginal Notes
Commentary on divine names from Ar-Raqib to Al-Hamid, with a marginal note on attributes of majesty and beauty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings and attribute categories of the remaining divine names and the marginal note's clarification on attributes of majesty and beauty.

Core Idea
Names like Ar-Raqib and Al-Wakil emphasize divine watchfulness and trust, while the marginal note resolves a potential redundancy in describing Allah.

The final set of names from Ar-Raqib to Al-Hamid includes key names like Al-Wakil, Al-Qawi, and Al-Matin. The lesson concludes with a marginal note discussing how to interpret the attributes of majesty (jalāl) and beauty (jamāl) to avoid confusion with the name Al-Mutakabbir.

Guided Path
1
Read the final names and definitions
Read the Arabic passage for the names from Ar-Raqib to Al-Hamid, noting their meanings and any alternative interpretations.
Al-Ghazali's comment on Ar-Raqib is notable for its depth.
2
Understand the marginal note
The marginal note discusses the interpretation of 'al-jalāl wa al-jamāl' attributes. Read it carefully and reflect on the commentator's advice to 'reflect' (tadabbar).
This shows the scholarly concern for precise theological language.
3
Review attribute categories
Identify the categories (real, active, negative, verbal, attributive) for each name in this lesson.
Many names here are active or refer to power.

( الغفار ) أي ( المريد لإزالة العقوبة عن مستحقها ) فهو راجع إلى صفة الإرادة واشتقاقه من الغفر بمعنى الستر ( القهار : غالب لا يغلب) فهو صفة فعلية سلبية (الوهاب : كثير العطاء بلا عوض فيكون صفة فعلية ( الرزاق : يرزق من يشاء ) من الحيوان ما ينتفع به من مأكول ومشروب وملبوس فهو من الفعل (الفتاح ميسر العسير وقيل : خالق الفتح أي (النصر) وهو على التقديرين راجع إلى الصفات الفعلية ( وقيل : الحاكم (وهو) أي الحكم (إما بالإخبار ) والقول فيكون صفة كلامية ( أو بالقضاء والقدر فيرجع إلى صفة القدرة والإرادة، والفتاح بمعنى الحاكم مشتق من الفتاحة وهي الحكم ( ومنه قوله تعالى : ربنا افتح بيننا وبين قومنا بالحق ﴾ [الأعراف : ۸۹] ، أي احكم وقيل : الحاكم) معناه ( المانع ومنه حكمة (اللجام وهي الحديدة المانعة من جماح الدابة فهو صفة فعلية ( العليم : العالم المعلومات ) فهو صفة حقيقية ( القابض : المختص بالسلب الباسط : المختص بجميع بالتوسعة ) في العطية .
