(المذل : الموجب لحط المنزلة ) فهذه كلها صفة فعلية ( السميع البصير ظاهر) معناهما مما سبق (الحكم الحاكم وقد عرفت معناه ومرجعه. (وقيل) الحكم ( هو الصحيح علمه وقوله وفعله فيرجع إلى هذه الصفات (العدل: لا يقبح منه ما يفعل ) فهو صفة سلبية ( اللطيف : خالق اللطف ( يلطف بعباده من حيث لا يعلمون ولا يحتسبون . وقيل : العالم بالخفيات ( فعلى الأول يرجع إلى الفعل وعلى الثاني إلى العلم ( الخبير) معناه (العليم) فصفة علمية . ( وقيل : المخير) فصفة كلامية ( الحليم : لا يعجل العقاب ) للعصاة قبل وقته المقدر فيرجع إلى السلب. (العظيم قد مر معناه في تفسيير الجبار ( الغفور كالغفار) بلا فرق على قياس الرحمن الرحيم ( الشكور : المجازي على الشكر فإن جزاء الشيء يسمى باسمه . ( وقيل : ) معناه أنه ( يثبت على القليل من الطاعة (الكثير) من النعمة وعلى التقديرين هو صفة فعلية ( وقيل ) معناه ( المثني على من أطاعه فيكون صفة كلامية العلي الكبير ) هما ( كالمتكبر في المعنى . ( الحفيظ ) معناه (العليم) من الذي هو ضد السهو والنسيان ومرجعه العلم . ( وقيل : من لا يشغله شيء عن شيء) فمرجعه صفة سلبية . وقيل : يبقي صور الأشياء) فصفة فعلية من الحفظ الذي يضاد التضييع ( المقيت : خالق الأقوات وقيل : المقدر فيرجع على التقديرين إلى الفعل ( وقيل : ( معناه ( الشهيد : هو العالم بالغائب والحاضر كما سيأتي في تفسيره الحفظ الكائنات فيرجع إلى العلم وقيل : المقتدر فيرجع إلى القدرة ( الحسيب، الكافي يخلق ما يكفي (العباد في مصالحهم ومهماتهم فهو صفة فعلية من قولهم: أكرمني فلان وأحسبني أي أعطاني حتى قلت حسبي . ( وقيل : المحاسب بأخباره المكلفين بما فعلوا ) من خير وشر فيرجع إلى صفة كلامية . ( الجليل كالمتكبر) وقيل : هو المتصف بصفة الجلال والجمال. الكريم ذو الجود وقيل : المتقدر على الجود) ومرجعهما الفعل والقدرة ( وقيل ) معناه ( العلي الرتبة ومنه كرائم المواشي ) لنفائسها فيرجع إلى صفة إضافية ( وقيل : يغفر الذنوب الرقيب كالحفيظ ) . وقال الغزالي : هو أخص من الحفيظ لأن الرقيب هو الذي يراعي الشيء بحيث لا يغفل عنه أصلا ويلاحظه ملاحظة دائمة لازمة لزوما لو عرفه الممنوع عن ذلك الشيء لما أقدم عليه فكأنه يرجع إلى العلم والحفظ ولكن باعتبار اللزوم وبالإضافة إلى ممنوع عنه محروس عن التناول . ( المجيب يجيب الأدعية . الواسع هو الذي وسع جوده جميع وعلمه جميع المعلومات وقدرته جميع المقدورات فلا يشغله شأن عن شأن. ( الحكيم العليم ذو اللحمة وهي العلم بالأشياء على ما هي عليه والإتيان بالأفعال على ما ينبغي، وقيل: الحكيم بمعنى المحكم من الأحكام وهو إتقان التدبير وإحسان التقدير ( الودود المودود) من الود وهو المحبة ) كالحلوب والركوب ) بمعنى المحلوب والمركوب ( وقيل : ( معناه الواد كالصبور بمعنى الصابر) أي يود ثناءه على المطيع وثوابه له المجيد الجميل أفعاله وقيل : الكثير أفضاله ، وقيل : لا يشارك فيما له من أوصاف المدح الباعث المعيد للخلائق يوم القيامة ( الشهيد العالم بالغائب والحاضر الحق) معناه ( العدل وقيل : الواجب لذاته أي لا يفتقر في وجوده إلى غيره . ( وقيل : ( معناه ( المحق أي الصادق ( في القول ( وقيل : مظهر الحق الوكيل المتكفل بأمور الخلق وحاجاتهم. ( وقيل : الموكول إِليه ذلك ) فإن عباده وكلوا إليه مصالحهم اعتماداً على إحسانه ( القوي، القادر على كل أمر، المتين) . قال الآمدي : معناه نفي النهاية في القدرة يعني أن قدرته لا تتناهى وفي عبارة الكتاب ( هي النهاية في القدرة ولا يبعد أن يكون تصحيفاً والأظهر أن يراد أن المتانة هي بلوغ القدرة ) إلى النهاية والغاية وذلك إذا كانت غير متناهية الولي : الحافظ للولاية ) أي النصرة فمعناه الناصر . ( وقيل : هو بمعنى المتولي للأمر والقائم به الحميد المحمود فهو قوله : (وقيل: هو المتصف بصفات الجلال والإكرام :قيل ينبغي أن يحمل صفة الجلال والجمال على صفة القهر واللطف حتى يكون مغايراً لمعنى المتكبر وإلا فإن أريد بهما الصفة السلبية والثبوتية كما قيل في تفسيرها لم يكن لقول الشارح وقيل إلخ فائدة، فتأمل .
Al-Ghaffar (the Oft-Forgiving): meaning the One who intends to remove punishment from its deserving ones, so it refers to the attribute of will, and its derivation is from 'ghafr' meaning concealment. Al-Qahhar (the Subduer): dominant, not dominated, so it is an active-negative attribute. Al-Wahhab (the Bestower): abundant giving without compensation, thus an active attribute. Ar-Razzaq (the Provider): provides for whom He wills of living beings with what benefits them from food, drink, and clothing, so it is from action. Al-Fattah (the Opener): the one who eases difficulty, and it is said: the creator of victory (i.e., triumph), and in both cases it refers to active attributes. And it is said: Al-Hakam (the Judge), and judgment is either by informing and speech, so it is a verbal attribute, or by decree and predestination, so it refers to the attributes of power and will. Al-Fattah in the sense of judge is derived from 'fattaha' which is judgment, and from it is His saying: 'Our Lord, judge between us and our people with truth' [Al-A'raf:89], i.e., judge. And it is said: Al-Hakam means the restrainer, and from it is 'hukmat al-lijam' (the bit) which is the iron piece that prevents the animal from bolting, so it is an active attribute. Al-'Alim (the All-Knowing): the knower of all known things, so it is a real attribute. Al-Qabid (the Constrictor): the one who withholds, Al-Basit (the Expander): the one who expands in giving. Al-Khafid (the Abaser): the repeller of affliction, from 'khafd' meaning lowering and placement. Ar-Rafi' (the Exalter): the giver of high stations. Al-Mu'izz (the Honorer): the giver of honor, and in most copies of the book 'the giver of strength', both are clear. Al-Mudhill (the Humiliator): the one who causes lowering of status. All these are active attributes. As-Sami' (the All-Hearing) and Al-Basir (the All-Seeing): their meanings are apparent from what preceded. Al-Hakam (the Judge): you have known its meaning and reference. And it is said: Al-Hakam is the one whose knowledge, speech, and action are correct, so it refers to these attributes. Al-'Adl (the Just): nothing disgraceful proceeds from Him, so it is a negative attribute. Al-Latif (the Subtle): the creator of subtle goodness, He is kind to His servants in ways they do not know or expect. And it is said: the knower of hidden things. On the first view it refers to action, on the second to knowledge. Al-Khabir (the All-Aware): its meaning is Al-'Alim, so it is an attribute of knowledge. And it is said: the one who gives good tidings, so it is a verbal attribute. Al-Halim (the Forbearing): does not hasten punishment to sinners before its appointed time, so it refers to negation. Al-'Azim (the Magnificent): its meaning has passed in the explanation of Al-Jabbar. Al-Ghafur (the Forgiving) is like Al-Ghaffar with no difference, on the analogy of Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim. Ash-Shakur (the Appreciative): the one who rewards for gratitude, for the recompense of a thing is named after it. And it is said: its meaning is that He gives much reward for a little obedience, and in both cases it is an active attribute. And it is said: its meaning is the one who praises those who obey Him, so it would be a verbal attribute. Al-'Ali (the Most High) and Al-Kabir (the Great): both are like Al-Mutakabbir in meaning. Al-Hafiz (the Preserver): its meaning is Al-'Alim, from that which is opposite of forgetfulness and negligence, and its reference is knowledge. And it is said: the one whom no thing occupies from another thing, so its reference is a negative attribute. And it is said: the one who preserves the forms of things, so it is an active attribute from preservation which is opposite of loss. Al-Muqit (the Nourisher): the creator of provisions, and it is said: the determiner, so in both views it refers to action. And it is said: its meaning is Ash-Shahid (the Witness): He is the knower of the unseen and the present, as will come in its interpretation. Al-Hifz (the Preservation) of beings, so it refers to knowledge. And it is said: Al-Muqtadir (the Powerful) refers to power. Al-Hasib (the Reckoner): the sufficient one who creates what suffices the servants in their interests and needs, so it is an active attribute, from their saying: 'So-and-so honored me and hasabani, i.e., gave me until I said 'hasbi' (enough for me).' And it is said: the one who calls His servants to account for what they have done of good and evil, so it refers to a verbal attribute. Al-Jalil (the Majestic): like Al-Mutakabbir, and it is said: the one described with the attributes of majesty and beauty. Al-Karim (the Generous): the possessor of generosity, and it is said: the one capable of generosity, and their reference is action and power. And it is said: its meaning is the exalted in rank, and from it is 'kara'im al-mawashi' (choice livestock) for their preciousness, so it refers to an attributive relation. And it is said: He forgives sins. Ar-Raqib (the Watchful): like Al-Hafiz. Al-Ghazali said: it is more specific than Al-Hafiz because Ar-Raqib is the one who watches a thing such that he never neglects it at all and observes it with a constant, necessary observation such that if the one forbidden from that thing knew of it, he would not proceed, so it seems to refer to knowledge and preservation, but with regard to necessity and in relation to someone forbidden from a thing guarded from handling. Al-Mujib (the Answerer of prayers): He answers supplications. Al-Wasi' (the Vast): He whose generosity encompasses all, His knowledge all known things, and His power all possible things, so no matter occupies Him from another. Al-Hakim (the Wise): Al-'Alim (the All-Knowing) possessing wisdom, which is knowledge of things as they are and performing actions as they ought. And it is said: Al-Hakim in the sense of Al-Muhkim (the Perfect) from 'ihkam' (perfection), which is precision of management and excellence of estimation. Al-Wadud (the Loving): the one who is loved, from 'wudd' which is love, like 'halub' and 'rakub' meaning the one milked and the one ridden. And it is said: its meaning is Al-Wad (the Lover) like 'sabur' meaning patient, i.e., He loves the praise of the obedient and His reward for him. Al-Majid (the Glorious): the beautiful in His actions, and it is said: the abundant in His bounties, and it is said: He has no partner in His attributes of praise. Al-Ba'ith (the Resurrector): the one who brings back creation on the Day of Resurrection. Ash-Shahid (the Witness): the knower of the unseen and the present. Al-Haqq (the Truth): its meaning is Al-'Adl (the Just), and it is said: the self-necessary, i.e., He does not need another in His existence. And it is said: its meaning is Al-Muhiqq (the Realizer), i.e., the truthful in speech. And it is said: the manifest of truth. Al-Wakil (the Trustee): the one who undertakes the affairs and needs of creation. And it is said: the one to whom that is entrusted, for His servants entrust their interests to Him relying on His beneficence. Al-Qawi (the Strong): the powerful over every matter. Al-Matin (the Firm): Al-Amidi said: its meaning is the negation of limit in power, meaning that His power is infinite. And in the wording of the book: 'It is the ultimate in power,' and it is not unlikely to be a corruption, and the apparent meaning is that 'matanah' is the reaching of power to the ultimate and extreme, which occurs when it is infinite. Al-Wali (the Guardian): the preserver of alliance, i.e., assistance, so its meaning is the Helper. And it is said: it means the one who undertakes the affair and stands by it. Al-Hamid (the Praiseworthy): the praised. [Marginal note]: His saying: 'And it is said: He is the one described with the attributes of majesty and honor' – it is said: the attributes of majesty and beauty should be interpreted as the attributes of subjugation and kindness so that they differ from the meaning of Al-Mutakabbir; otherwise, if they are intended as negative and positive attributes as is said in their interpretation, then the commentator's saying 'and it is said' etc. would have no benefit. So reflect.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a detailed lexical and theological commentary on the divine names (al-asmā' al-ḥusnā). The author proceeds name by name, explaining each name's literal meaning, its derivation, and its categorization among the divine attributes. The overall pattern is as follows: For each name, the author gives a definition (often citing alternative views), then classifies it as either a ṣifa ḥaqīqiyya (real attribute, e.g., knowledge, power), ṣifa fiʿliyya (active attribute, e.g., providing, forgiving), ṣifa salbiyya (negative attribute, e.g., not unjust, not negligent), or ṣifa kalāmiyya (verbal attribute, e.g., judging by speech). Occasionally, he notes when a name can be understood in multiple ways, each leading to a different attribute category. The commentary draws on linguistic analysis and Quranic usage. For example, al-Ghaffār is linked to the will to remove punishment, making it a volitional attribute. Al-Qahhār is active-negative because it implies dominance over others. Al-ʿAlīm is a real attribute because knowledge is inherent. The passage also includes notable opinions from earlier scholars like al-Ghazālī, particularly on the distinction between al-Raqīb and al-Ḥafīẓ. The marginal note at the end discusses a subtle point: how to interpret 'al-Jalāl' and 'al-Jamāl' to avoid redundancy with al-Mutakabbir. The author advises reflecting on the commentator's phrasing. Overall, the passage demonstrates the method of tawqīfī (revelation-based) naming: each name is taken from revelation and explained rationally in terms of its theological import.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the attributes of majesty (jalāl) and beauty (jamāl) are redundant with the name Al-Mutakabbir.
Correction: They should be interpreted as subjugation and kindness, distinguishing them from Al-Mutakabbir.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali's comment in the passage, how is Ar-Raqib different from Al-Hafiz?
A) Ar-Raqib is more general
B) Ar-Raqib involves constant watchfulness with a necessary observation
C) Ar-Raqib refers only to knowledge
D) Ar-Raqib is a negative attribute

Key Takeaway
The final names emphasize divine power, watchfulness, and trust, while the marginal note clarifies that attributes of majesty and beauty must be distinguished from the name Al-Mutakabbir.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as Ar-Raqib, do you truly believe that His watchfulness should prevent you from sinning even when no one sees you? What specific action will you change today because of this awareness?
?
You say 'Hasbunallahu wa ni'mal wakil' (Sufficient for us is Allah, and He is the best Trustee). In what area of your life are you still relying on your own planning rather than entrusting your affairs to Al-Wakil? Name one decision this week that you will consciously surrender to Him.
?
The marginal note warns against redundancy in describing Allah. When you praise Allah with attributes, do you ever repeat words without contemplating their meaning? How can you ensure that your praise is from a heart that reflects on each name's distinct significance?

Al-Kainat and Al-Hasib: Knowledge, Power, and Active Attributes
Defines Al-Kainat and Al-Hasib with multiple interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine names Al-Kainat and Al-Hasib have multiple interpretations, each linking to specific attributes like knowledge, power, action, and speech.

Core Idea
Divine names reveal multiple facets of divine action and speech.

In this lesson, we explore the first two divine names mentioned in the text: Al-Kainat (the Creator of things) and Al-Hasib (the Sufficient). Each name is given multiple interpretations that connect to fundamental divine attributes. By examining these interpretations, we learn how kalam scholars classify attributes as active, verbal, or related to knowledge and power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the text carefully to understand the flow and the multiple interpretations given for each name.
2
Identify interpretations for Al-Kainat
Find the two interpretations: one referring to knowledge, another to power.
Note that both are based on different linguistic angles.
3
Analyze Al-Hasib's meanings
Distinguish between the active attribute (sufficing) and the verbal attribute (informing).
The active attribute involves creation, the verbal attribute involves speech.
4
Connect interpretations to attribute categories
Reflect on how each interpretation anchors back to a standard attribute type: knowledge, power, active, verbal.
5
Ponder the multiplicity of interpretations
Consider why scholars might offer multiple valid meanings for the same divine name.
This shows the richness and depth of Allah's names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكائنات فيرجع إلى العلم وقيل : المقتدر فيرجع إلى القدرة ( الحسيب، الكافي يخلق ما يكفي (العباد في مصالحهم ومهماتهم فهو صفة فعلية من قولهم: أكرمني فلان وأحسبني أي أعطاني حتى قلت حسبي . ( وقيل : المحاسب بأخباره المكلفين بما فعلوا ) من خير وشر فيرجع إلى صفة كلامية . ( الجليل كالمتكبر) وقيل : هو المتصف بصفة الجلال والجمال. الكريم ذو الجود وقيل : المتقدر على الجود) ومرجعهما الفعل والقدرة ( وقيل ) معناه ( العلي الرتبة ومنه كرائم المواشي ) لنفائسها فيرجع إلى صفة إضافية ( وقيل : يغفر الذنوب
Al-Kainat (the Creator of things) then it refers to knowledge, and it is said: Al-Muqtadir (the Powerful) refers to power. Al-Hasib (the Reckoner), Al-Kafi (the Sufficient) creates what suffices for the servants in their interests and needs, so it is an active attribute from their saying: So-and-so honored me and sufficed me, i.e., he gave me until I said 'enough'. And it is said: the Reckoner who informs the servants of what they have done of good and evil, so it refers to a verbal attribute. Al-Jalil (the Majestic) like Al-Mutakabbir (the Proud), and it is said: He is the one described with majesty and beauty. Al-Karim (the Generous) possesses generosity, and it is said: the powerful over generosity, and both refer to action and power. And it is said: its meaning is the high rank, and from it are the precious livestock for their value, so it refers to an additional attribute. And it is said: He forgives sins.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage defines the first four divine names mentioned in the text. For each name, multiple interpretations are given, and each is categorized as one of the standard attribute types in kalam: active (fi'liyya), verbal (kalamiyya), or additional (idafiyya). The name Al-Kainat (الكائنات) is interpreted as referring to knowledge (علم) or power (قدرة). Al-Hasib (الحسيب) has two meanings: one as an active attribute (sufficing creation), and another as a verbal attribute (informing of deeds). Al-Jalil (الجليل) is compared to Al-Mutakabbir and described with both majesty and beauty. Al-Karim (الكريم) is explained as having generosity and power over generosity, and also as being of high rank, which is an additional attribute. The passage ends with a final interpretation of Al-Karim as 'He forgives sins.' The commentary follows the order of the source, explaining each name's possible meanings and the type of attribute it is associated with.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think each divine name has only one fixed meaning.
Correction: The passage shows that reputable scholars offer multiple interpretations for the same name, each grounded in a different attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which attribute does the interpretation of Al-Hasib as Al-Kafi (the Sufficient) refer to?
A) Knowledge
B) Power
C) An active attribute
D) A verbal attribute

Key Takeaway
The names Al-Kainat and Al-Hasib illustrate that divine names can have multiple interpretations linked to distinct attributes like knowledge, power, action, and speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as Al-Hasib, do you truly trust that He is enough for every need, or do you still cling to worldly guarantees? What is one area of your life where you need to say 'hasbi Allah' (Allah is sufficient for me) and let go of your own planning today?
?
The name Al-Kainat can mean the Creator (pointing to Allah's knowledge) or the All-Powerful (pointing to His power). Which aspect do you neglect more in your daily thoughts? How can you consciously praise Him for both His knowledge and His power in your next prayer?
?
If Allah's attribute of speech includes informing you of your deeds, what deeds will you be ashamed to hear about? Knowing that Al-Hasib will recount everything, what change must you start making tonight?

Al-Jalil and Al-Karim: Majesty, Generosity, and Additional Attributes
Defines Al-Jalil and Al-Karim with multiple interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple interpretations of the divine names Al-Jalil and Al-Karim, and how they relate to action, power, and additional attributes.

Core Idea
Majesty and generosity are expressed through multiple divine attributes.

In this lesson, we turn to the names Al-Jalil (the Majestic) and Al-Karim (the Generous). Each name carries several meanings that link to attributes of action, power, and relation. We will see how Al-Jalil is associated with majesty and beauty, and Al-Karim with generosity, power over generosity, high rank, and forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage for Al-Jalil and Al-Karim
Read the text carefully, noting the different interpretations given for each name.
2
Analyze Al-Jalil
Identify the two interpretations: like Al-Mutakabbir, and described with majesty and beauty. Determine which attributes these refer to.
3
Analyze Al-Karim
Note the three interpretations: possessing generosity / powerful over generosity (action and power), high rank (additional attribute), and forgiving sins.
Pay attention to how each interpretation is categorized.
4
Understand attribute categories
Notice that some interpretations refer to action and power (صفة فعلية and قدرة), while one refers to an additional attribute (صفة إضافية).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكائنات فيرجع إلى العلم وقيل : المقتدر فيرجع إلى القدرة ( الحسيب، الكافي يخلق ما يكفي (العباد في مصالحهم ومهماتهم فهو صفة فعلية من قولهم: أكرمني فلان وأحسبني أي أعطاني حتى قلت حسبي . ( وقيل : المحاسب بأخباره المكلفين بما فعلوا ) من خير وشر فيرجع إلى صفة كلامية . ( الجليل كالمتكبر) وقيل : هو المتصف بصفة الجلال والجمال. الكريم ذو الجود وقيل : المتقدر على الجود) ومرجعهما الفعل والقدرة ( وقيل ) معناه ( العلي الرتبة ومنه كرائم المواشي ) لنفائسها فيرجع إلى صفة إضافية ( وقيل : يغفر الذنوب
Al-Kainat (the Creator of things) then it refers to knowledge, and it is said: Al-Muqtadir (the Powerful) refers to power. Al-Hasib (the Reckoner), Al-Kafi (the Sufficient) creates what suffices for the servants in their interests and needs, so it is an active attribute from their saying: So-and-so honored me and sufficed me, i.e., he gave me until I said 'enough'. And it is said: the Reckoner who informs the servants of what they have done of good and evil, so it refers to a verbal attribute. Al-Jalil (the Majestic) like Al-Mutakabbir (the Proud), and it is said: He is the one described with majesty and beauty. Al-Karim (the Generous) possesses generosity, and it is said: the powerful over generosity, and both refer to action and power. And it is said: its meaning is the high rank, and from it are the precious livestock for their value, so it refers to an additional attribute. And it is said: He forgives sins.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage defines the first four divine names mentioned in the text. For each name, multiple interpretations are given, and each is categorized as one of the standard attribute types in kalam: active (fi'liyya), verbal (kalamiyya), or additional (idafiyya). The name Al-Kainat (الكائنات) is interpreted as referring to knowledge (علم) or power (قدرة). Al-Hasib (الحسيب) has two meanings: one as an active attribute (sufficing creation), and another as a verbal attribute (informing of deeds). Al-Jalil (الجليل) is compared to Al-Mutakabbir and described with both majesty and beauty. Al-Karim (الكريم) is explained as having generosity and power over generosity, and also as being of high rank, which is an additional attribute. The passage ends with a final interpretation of Al-Karim as 'He forgives sins.' The commentary follows the order of the source, explaining each name's possible meanings and the type of attribute it is associated with.

Al-Raqib and Al-Hafiz: The Distinction of Constant Watchfulness
Al-Ghazali's explanation of Al-Raqib as more specific than Al-Hafiz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific nuance that distinguishes Al-Raqib (the Watchful) from Al-Hafiz (the Preserver) according to Al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
Al-Raqib is the watchful one who never forgets, making his watchfulness more specific than preservation.

Al-Ghazali offers a subtle distinction between two seemingly similar divine names: Al-Raqib and Al-Hafiz. While both imply preservation, Al-Raqib emphasizes a constant, never-forgetting observation that deters wrongdoing. This lesson explores that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the initial comparison between Al-Raqib and Al-Hafiz.
2
Identify Al-Ghazali's distinction
Notice how Al-Ghazali differentiates Al-Raqib from Al-Hafiz using the concept of 'constant watchfulness' that deters prohibited actions.
3
Reflect on the effect
Consider the practical impact: if the prohibited person knew of this watchfulness, they would refrain from sin.
4
Connect to underlying attributes
Understand that this attribute ultimately traces back to knowledge and preservation, but with a specific relation to prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرقيب كالحفيظ . وقال الغزالي : هو أخص من الحفيظ لأن الرقيب هو الذي يراعي الشيء بحيث لا يغفل عنه أصلا ويلاحظه ملاحظة دائمة لازمة لزوما لو عرفه الممنوع عن ذلك الشيء لما أقدم عليه فكأنه يرجع إلى العلم والحفظ ولكن باعتبار اللزوم وبالإضافة إلى ممنوع عنه محروس عن التناول . ( المجيب يجيب الأدعية . الواسع هو الذي وسع جوده جميع وعلمه جميع المعلومات وقدرته جميع المقدورات فلا يشغله شأن عن شأن. ( الحكيم العليم ذو اللحمة وهي العلم بالأشياء على ما هي عليه والإتيان بالأفعال على ما ينبغي، وقيل: الحكيم بمعنى المحكم من الأحكام وهو إتقان التدبير وإحسان التقدير
Al-Raqib (the Watchful) is like Al-Hafiz (the Preserver). Al-Ghazali said: He is more specific than Al-Hafiz because Al-Raqib is the one who watches a thing such that he never forgets about it at all, and observes it with a constant, necessary observation such that if the one prohibited from that thing knew of it, he would not proceed. So it seems it refers to knowledge and preservation, but in terms of necessity and in relation to something prohibited that is guarded from being handled. Al-Mujib (the Answerer) answers prayers. Al-Wasi' (the Vast) is the one whose generosity encompasses all, whose knowledge encompasses all known things, and whose power encompasses all possible things, so no matter preoccupies Him from another. Al-Hakim (the Wise) is the Knower, possessor of wisdom? (ذو اللحمة) which is knowledge of things as they are and performing actions as they ought, and it is said: Al-Hakim means the one who perfects from the rulings, which is the perfection of management and excellence of estimation.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage defines five divine names. Al-Raqib is compared to Al-Hafiz, but Al-Ghazali specifies that Al-Raqib is more specific, involving constant watchfulness that prevents transgression. Al-Mujib is the one who answers prayers. Al-Wasi' emphasizes the vastness of His generosity, knowledge, and power. Al-Hakim is interpreted as the Knower, possessing wisdom (or 'luhma'?) and also as the one who perfectly manages and estimates. The commentary follows the order, explaining each name's nuance.

Key Terms
الرقيب
الحفيظ
العلم
الحفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Raqib and Al-Hafiz are exactly synonymous.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that Al-Raqib is more specific, involving constant watchfulness that deters, whereas Al-Hafiz may be broader.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ghazali, what makes Al-Raqib more specific than Al-Hafiz?
A) Al-Raqib refers to power, not knowledge
B) Al-Raqib involves constant watchfulness that deters prohibited actions
C) Al-Raqib only applies to angels
D) They are the same

Key Takeaway
Al-Raqib is the Watchful whose undivided attention deters sin, highlighting a more intense aspect of divine preservation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are alone and tempted by a sin, does your belief in Al-Raqib truly deter you? If not, what does that reveal about the state of your heart?
?
If you truly believed that Allah never forgets about your deeds, how would your daily habits change starting tomorrow?
?
What is one area of your life where you act as if Allah is not watching? What would it cost you to realign that area with the reality of Al-Raqib?

Definitions of Al-Mujib, Al-Wasi', and Al-Hakim.

Al-Wadud: The Loving and Beloved
Interpretation of the divine name Al-Wadud with linguistic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dual meaning of Al-Wadud as both loving and beloved, and its linguistic background.

Core Idea
Al-Wadud is both the Lover and the Beloved.

The name Al-Wadud appears in the Qur'an and is often understood as simply 'the Loving'. However, this passage reveals a deeper linguistic nuance: the word comes from 'wudd' (love) and can be interpreted in both active and passive senses. Let's examine the original text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage for Al-Wadud and its literal translation. Notice the examples of halub and rakub.
These examples illustrate the passive form.
2
Understand the two interpretations
The passage first explains Al-Wadud as 'al-mawdud' (the beloved), then suggests 'al-wad' (the lover).
Both are valid interpretations.
3
Reflect on the meaning
Consider why God would be described as both loving and beloved. How does this affect your understanding of divine love?
This enriches the concept of divine love.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الودود المودود) من الود وهو المحبة ) كالحلوب والركوب ) بمعنى المحلوب والمركوب ( وقيل : ( معناه الواد كالصبور بمعنى الصابر) أي يود ثناءه على المطيع وثوابه له المجيد الجميل أفعاله وقيل : الكثير أفضاله ، وقيل : لا يشارك فيما له من أوصاف المدح الباعث المعيد للخلائق يوم القيامة ( الشهيد العالم بالغائب والحاضر الحق) معناه ( العدل وقيل : الواجب لذاته أي لا يفتقر في وجوده إلى غيره . ( وقيل : ( معناه ( المحق أي الصادق ( في القول ( وقيل : مظهر الحق الوكيل المتكفل بأمور الخلق وحاجاتهم. ( وقيل : الموكول إِليه ذلك ) فإن عباده وكلوا إليه مصالحهم اعتماداً على إحسانه
Al-Wadud (the Loving) is the one who is loved (al-mawdud), from al-wudd which is love, like al-halub (being milked) and al-rakub (being ridden) meaning the one that is milked and the one that is ridden. And it is said: its meaning is al-wad (the lover) like al-sabur (patient) meaning patient, i.e., He loves the praise of the obedient and His reward to him. Al-Majid (the Glorious) is the one with beautiful acts, and it is said: the one of abundant favors, and it is said: no one shares in the attributes of praise that He possesses. Al-Ba'ith (the Resurrector) is the one who returns creation on the Day of Resurrection. Al-Shahid (the Witness) is the Knower of the absent and the present. Al-Haqq (the Truth) has meanings: justice, and it is said: the self-necessary, i.e., He does not need another in His existence. And it is said: its meaning is al-muhiqq (the one who makes truth firm), i.e., the truthful in speech, and it is said: the manifestor of truth. Al-Wakil (the Disposer) is the one who undertakes the affairs of creation and their needs. And it is said: the one to whom that is entrusted, for His servants entrust their interests to Him, relying on His goodness.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage defines six divine names. Al-Wadud is explained as both loving and beloved, with examples from passive forms. Al-Majid is described as having beautiful acts and abundant favors, unique in praise. Al-Ba'ith specifically refers to resurrection. Al-Shahid emphasizes knowledge of all. Al-Haqq has multiple interpretations: justice, self-necessity, truthfulness, and manifesting truth. Al-Wakil is understood as the one who manages creation's affairs. The commentary follows the order, noting the different interpretations for each.

Key Terms

المودود
الود
المحبة
الحلوب
الركوب
الواد
الصبور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Wadud only means 'the Loving' in an active sense.
Correction: The passage shows that it can also mean 'the Beloved' (passive), and both are valid.

Checkpoint
What does the example of 'al-halub' illustrate?
A) An active participle meaning 'milk-giving'
B) A passive meaning despite active form
C) A superlative form
D) A verbal noun

Key Takeaway
Al-Wadud can mean both the Lover and the Beloved, reflecting the mutual relationship of love between Allah and His servants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Al-Ba'ith: The Resurrector of Creation
Definition of Al-Ba'ith as the resurrector.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Al-Ba'ith specifically refers to Allah's act of resurrecting creation on the Day of Judgment.

Core Idea
Al-Ba'ith is the One who brings creation back to life.

Al-Ba'ith is one of the divine names related to the resurrection. This brief but powerful definition ties the name specifically to the event of raising the dead for judgment. Let's look at the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for Al-Ba'ith. Note the single definition.
2
Focus on the verb 'yu'id'
The word 'al-mu'id' (the restorer) emphasizes the return of creation after death.
This is a specific theological role.
3
Connect to eschatology
How does this name relate to Islamic teachings about the afterlife?
It affirms bodily resurrection.

Al-Wakil (the Disposer) is the one who undertakes the affairs of creation and their needs. And it is said: the one to whom that is entrusted, for His servants entrust their interests to Him, relying on His goodness.

Al-Haqq: Justice, Necessity, and Truth
Multiple interpretations of the divine name Al-Haqq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple interpretations of Al-Haqq, including justice, necessary being, truthfulness, and manifesting truth.

Core Idea
Al-Haqq has at least four profound meanings.

The name Al-Haqq is often simply translated as 'the Truth'. However, this passage reveals a rich tapestry of meanings: justice, self-necessity, truthfulness, and the manifestor of truth. Let's unpack these layers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the several 'qila' statements indicating different interpretations.
2
List the four meanings
The meanings are: 1) justice (al-'adl), 2) self-necessary (al-wajib li dhatihi), 3) al-muhiqq (the one who makes truth firm), 4) mazhir al-haqq (manifestor of truth).
3
Connect to theology
How does the meaning of 'self-necessary' relate to proofs of God's existence?
It is a key concept in kalam.

Al-Qawi, Al-Matin, Al-Wali, Al-Hamid: Power and Protection
Defines four divine names and a footnote on interpreting majesty and beauty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of four divine names—Al-Qawi, Al-Matin, Al-Wali, Al-Hamid—and the significance of interpreting majesty and beauty attributes as force and gentleness.

Core Idea
Allah's names Al-Qawi and Al-Matin emphasize infinite power, while Al-Wali and Al-Hamid highlight protection and praise.

This passage defines four of Allah’s beautiful names, each revealing a distinct perfection. Alongside these definitions, a careful note on the attributes of majesty and beauty prevents confusion with other names like Al-Mutakabbir. Let's explore these meanings and the subtle theological clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how each name is defined in sequence.
The passage includes a footnote on majesty and beauty.
2
Focus on Al-Matin
Pay attention to al-Amidi's explanation: Al-Matin means negation of limit in power. Understand why this corrects a possible textual corruption.
This clarifies that Allah's power is literally infinite.
3
Understand Al-Wali and Al-Hamid
Al-Wali is the protector/helper, and also the one who undertakes affairs. Al-Hamid is the praised one, an additional attribute.
These names convey Allah's care and worthiness of praise.
4
Examine the footnote on majesty and beauty
Read the footnote about attributes of majesty and beauty. Why does the commentator suggest interpreting them as force and gentleness?
This distinction prevents overlap with the name Al-Mutakabbir.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how these four names collectively affirm Allah's absolute power, protection, and praiseworthiness, and how the footnote safeguards precise theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القوي، القادر على كل أمر، المتين) . قال الآمدي : معناه نفي النهاية في القدرة يعني أن قدرته لا تتناهى وفي عبارة الكتاب ( هي النهاية في القدرة ولا يبعد أن يكون تصحيفاً والأظهر أن يراد أن المتانة هي بلوغ القدرة ) إلى النهاية والغاية وذلك إذا كانت غير متناهية الولي : الحافظ للولاية ) أي النصرة فمعناه الناصر . ( وقيل : هو بمعنى المتولي للأمر والقائم به الحميد المحمود فهو صفة إضافية

قوله : (وقيل: هو المتصف بصفات الجلال والإكرام :قيل ينبغي أن يحمل صفة الجلال والجمال على صفة القهر واللطف حتى يكون مغايراً لمعنى المتكبر وإلا فإن أريد بهما الصفة السلبية والثبوتية كما قيل في تفسيرها لم يكن لقول الشارح وقيل إلخ فائدة، فتأمل .
Al-Qawi (the Strong), the powerful over every thing. Al-Matin (the Firm). Al-Amidi said: its meaning is the negation of limit in power, meaning that His power does not end. And in the expression of the book: 'it is the limit in power' and it is not far that it is a corruption, and the more apparent is that firmness (al-matana) is the reaching of power to the limit and extreme, and that is when it is unlimited. Al-Wali (the Protector) is the preserver of protection (al-wilaya), i.e., victory, so its meaning is the Helper. And it is said: he is the one who undertakes the affair and maintains it. Al-Hamid (the Praiseworthy) is the one praised, so it is an additional attribute.

His saying: 'And it is said: He is described with attributes of majesty and generosity.' It is said: It is appropriate to carry the attributes of majesty and beauty on the attributes of force and gentleness so that it is different from the meaning of Al-Mutakabbir. Otherwise, if what is meant by them are the negative and positive attributes as said in their interpretation, then the commentator's saying 'and it is said' etc. would have no benefit, so consider.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage defines four names. Al-Qawi is simply powerful over all. Al-Matin is given a detailed explanation by al-Amidi, interpreting it as the negation of limit in power, clarifying a possible textual corruption. Al-Wali means protector or helper. Al-Hamid means praised and is classified as an additional attribute. The passage then includes a footnote discussing the interpretation of 'majesty and generosity' and whether they should be understood as force and gentleness to distinguish from Al-Mutakabbir. The commentary explains the reasoning.

Key Terms
القوي
المتين
الولي
الحميد
الآمدي
النهاية
القدرة
الجلال
الجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Matin might be misunderstood as merely 'firm' in a physical sense, implying limitation.
Correction: Al-Matin actually means negation of limit in power, i.e., infinite power without end.

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi, what does Al-Matin (المتين) mean?
A) Perfect in every way
B) Negation of limit in power
C) Firm in resolve
D) Possessing extreme strength

Key Takeaway
Al-Matin means unlimited power, and the attributes of majesty and beauty are best understood as force and gentleness to distinguish them from other names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is Al-Qawi (the Strong), why do you often rely on your own strength instead of turning to Him? What is one area of your life where you are trusting your power over His, and what will you do today to actively depend on Him?
?
Al-Wali is the Protector and Helper. When was the last time you truly felt helpless and called upon His protection? Are there fears you are holding onto that you have not surrendered to Al-Wali? Write down one fear and make a sincere du'a right now.
?
The commentator warns against misinterpreting Allah's attributes. Is there an attribute of Allah that you have misunderstood, leading you to have a flawed relationship with Him? How can you correct that today based on this lesson?

Seven Divine Names: Counting, Creation, Life, and Self-Subsistence
Enumerates seven divine names with definitions and categorization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of seven divine names and how they are categorized as self-attributes or active attributes.

Core Idea
God's names reveal His essence as well as His actions toward creation.

In this passage, Sanusi defines seven of the most beautiful names of Allah, each highlighting a different aspect of His relationship with creation. Some names point to attributes inherent in His essence, while others describe His ongoing acts. Pay attention to how each name is explained and which category it falls into.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text aloud to get a feel for the definitions. Notice the use of 'قيل' (it is said) indicating multiple interpretations.
2
Identify each name and its definition
List the seven names: Al-Muhsi, Al-Mubdi', Al-Mu'id, Al-Muhyi, Al-Mumit, Al-Hayy, Al-Qayyum. For each, note the definition given.
Some names have more than one interpretation.
3
Categorize as self-attribute or active attribute
The passage explicitly categorizes Al-Qayyum as both self-attribute (صفة نفسية) and active (صفة فعلية). Try to deduce categories for the others based on their definitions.
Self-attributes describe God's essence; active attributes describe His actions.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these names are important for understanding God's relationship with creation. How does 'Al-Muhsi' (Enumerator) relate to divine knowledge?
The names highlight God's knowledge, power, and sustenance.

Source Text

And it is said: the Powerful, and from it is known that 'you cannot enumerate them' meaning you cannot bear them. Al-Mubdi' (the Beginner) is the one who shows favor by beginning the blessings. Commentary

Al-Mu'id restores creation after death. Al-Muhyi and Al-Mumit are creators of life and death. Al-Hayy is stated as clear from earlier. Al-Qayyum is explained as eternal (self-attribute) and as manager of all creatures (active attribute). The commentary notes the categorization of each name.

?

Al-Wajid and Al-Majid: Self-Sufficiency and Glory
Defines Al-Wajid and Al-Majid with their meanings and attribute classifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names Al-Wajid (the Self-Sufficient) and Al-Majid (the Glorious), including their classifications as negative, additional, or active attributes.

Core Idea
Al-Wajid means self-sufficient; Al-Majid means glorious—both highlight Allah's independence and majesty.

The passage introduces two of Allah's names. Al-Wajid is primarily understood as the Self-Sufficient, a negative attribute negating need. Al-Majid is associated with highness and glory, an additional attribute. Understanding these classifications deepens our comprehension of Allah's perfections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for Al-Wajid and Al-Majid
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the different interpretations.
2
Identify the types of attributes
Al-Wajid's first meaning is a negative attribute (self-sufficient). Al-Majid is an additional attribute (high). Also note the alternative interpretation of Al-Wajid as knower (additional) and Al-Majid as active attribute (protection).
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Why does it matter that Allah is self-sufficient and glorious? How do these attributes relate to tawhid?
Connect to the broader Islamic concept of Allah's independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواجد الغني) أي الذي لا يفتقر فهو صفة سلبية . ( وقيل : ( معناه ( العالم الماجد العالي ) فهو صفة إضافية. (وقيل: من له الولاية والتولية) فيكون صفة فعلية ( الأحد قد مر تفسيره أي علم ذلك مما سبق أنه في وحدانيته من يمتنع أن يشاركه شيء في ماهيته وصفات كماله، وقد يروى الواحد بدل الأحد ويفرق بينهما فيقال : هو إحدى الذات أي لا تركيب فيه وأوحد الصفات لا مشارك له فيها ( الصمد) معناه (السيد ) وهو المالك فيكون صفة إضافية . ( وقيل) معناه ( الحليم ) أي الذي لا يستفزه ولا تقلقه أفعال العصاة فتكون صفة سلبية ( وقيل : العالي الدرجة ، وقيل : المدعو المسؤول) الذي يصمد لقضاء الحوائج وعلى التقديرين هو صفة إضافية ( وقيل : الصمد ما لا جوف له أي المصمت فداله مبدلة من التاء وحاصله نفي التركيب وقبول الانقسام.
Al-Wajid (the Finder) is the Self-Sufficient, i.e., the one who does not need, so it is a negative attribute. And it is said: its meaning is the Knower. Al-Majid (the Glorious) is the High, so it is an additional attribute. And it is said: the one who has protection and delegation, so it is an active attribute. Al-Ahad (the One) — its interpretation has passed, i.e., that is known from what preceded: that in His oneness, nothing can share in His essence and attributes of perfection. Sometimes Al-Wahid is narrated instead of Al-Ahad, and they are distinguished: Al-Ahad refers to the oneness of essence, i.e., no composition in Him, and Al-Wahid refers to the oneness of attributes, that there is no partner for Him. Al-Samad (the Eternal) means the Master, and He is the Owner, so it is an additional attribute. And it is said: its meaning is Al-Halim (the Forbearing), i.e., the one whom the actions of the disobedient do not agitate or disturb, so it is a negative attribute. And it is said: the High in degree, and it is said: the one invoked, the one asked, the one to whom one turns for fulfilling needs, and in both cases it is an additional attribute. And it is said: Al-Samad is what has no cavity, i.e., solid, its 'dal' is a substitute for 'ta', and its result is negation of composition and division.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines four names. Al-Wajid is explained as self-sufficient (negative attribute) or knower (additional). Al-Majid is explained as high or having protection. Al-Ahad is discussed in detail, distinguishing between Ahad and Wahid: Ahad negates composition in essence, Wahid negates partners in attributes. Al-Samad has multiple interpretations: master (additional), forbearing (negative), high degree, the one invoked, or solid without cavity (negating composition). The commentary follows the order, highlighting the distinctions.

Explains Al-Ahad and Al-Samad with multiple interpretations and distinctions.

Eight Divine Names: Power, Priority, Eternity, and Manifestness
Defines eight divine names in four pairs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings and classifications of eight divine names: al-Qadir, al-Muqtadir, al-Muqaddim, al-Mu'akhkhir, al-Awwal, al-Akhir, al-Zahir, and al-Batin.

Core Idea
Four pairs of names reveal divine power, will, eternity, and hiddenness.

This lesson explores eight names of Allah, each revealing a different facet of His majesty. The author presents them in pairs, explaining their meanings and whether they are negative, active, or additional attributes. Understanding these names deepens our knowledge of Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice how each pair is introduced and defined succinctly.
The style is concise; each name is followed by a brief definition.
2
Identify each pair and their key features
There are four pairs: Qadir/Muqtadir, Muqaddim/Mu'akhkhir, Awwal/Akhir, Zahir/Batin. For each, note what attribute is described and any special emphasis.
For example, Muqtadir is more emphatic than Qadir.
3
Note the classification of each name
The author classifies some as negative attributes (Awwal, Akhir, and one interpretation of Batin) and others as additional or active. Determine which category each falls into.
Negative attributes negate imperfection; additional attributes add a positive meaning.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these names are important for understanding Allah. For instance, al-Zahir and al-Batin show that Allah is both known through proofs and hidden from senses.
This pairs with the common misunderstanding about visibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القادر المقتدر) كلاهما ( ظاهر) والثاني أبلغ من الأول ( المقدم المؤخر يقدم من يشاء ويؤخر من يشاء الأول الآخر لم يزل ولا يزال ) أي أنه قبل كل شيء وليس قبله شيء وبعد كل شيء وليس بعده شيء فهما صفتان سلبيتان ( الظاهر المعلوم بالأدلة القاطعة ) فهو صفة إضافية :( وقيل : الغالب ) فصفة فعلية من ظهر فلان على فلان أي قهره ( الباطن المحتجب عن الحواس ( بحيث لا تدركه أصلاً فيكون صفة سلبية . ( وقيل : العالم بالخفيات
Al-Qadir (the Powerful) and Al-Muqtadir (the All-Determining) — both are apparent, and the second is more emphatic than the first. Al-Muqaddim (the Bringer Forward) and Al-Mu'akhkhir (the Postponer) — He brings forward whom He wills and postpones whom He wills. Al-Awwal (the First) and Al-Akhir (the Last) — He has not ceased and will not cease, i.e., He is before everything and nothing is before Him, and after everything and nothing is after Him, so they are two negative attributes. Al-Zahir (the Manifest) is known through decisive proofs, so it is an additional attribute. And it is said: the Overcomer, an active attribute, from 'zahara' meaning to overcome. Al-Batin (the Hidden) is the one concealed from the senses such that they do not perceive Him at all, so it is a negative attribute. And it is said: the Knower of hidden things.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines eight names. Al-Qadir and Al-Muqtadir differ in emphasis. Al-Muqaddim and Al-Mu'akhkhir are active attributes of volition. Al-Awwal and Al-Akhir are negative attributes indicating eternity without beginning or end. Al-Zahir is known through proofs (additional attribute) or overcoming (active). Al-Batin is hidden from senses (negative) or knower of hidden (additional). The commentary explains each.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Zahir means Allah is physically visible or observable.
Correction: The passage clarifies that al-Zahir is 'known through decisive proofs' (additional attribute) or 'the Overcomer' (active attribute), not visible to the eye.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation beyond your control, do you genuinely trust that Allah is al-Muqtadir (the All-Determining), or does anxiety reveal a hidden belief that your own planning is what truly determines outcomes? Today, identify one area where you must surrender to His power.
?
How often do you complain about delays in your life—waiting for marriage, children, success? The name al-Mu'akhkhir (the Postponer) reminds you that Allah's timing is intentional. Will you stop resenting His postponements and instead ask Him to teach you patience in the wait?
?
Allah is al-Batin (the Hidden), concealed from your senses. Do you live as if He does not see your private thoughts and secret sins? How would your inner world change if you remembered that He hears your whispers and knows what you try to hide—not to punish, but to call you back to Him?

Al-Wali and Al-Muta'ali: The Owner and the Supreme
Definition of two names: al-Wali and al-Muta'ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of Al-Wali (the Protector) and Al-Muta'ali (the Supreme) as defined in the text.

Core Idea
Al-Wali is the Owner, and Al-Muta'ali is the Supreme with emphasis.

This passage introduces two names of Allah that emphasize His ownership and exaltedness. Al-Wali is explained simply as 'the Owner', while Al-Muta'ali is likened to Al-'Ali with a nuance of intensity. Understanding these names deepens our reverence for Allah's absolute authority and transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic phrase for Al-Wali and Al-Muta'ali, then its literal translation. Notice the concise definition style.
2
Examine the meaning of Al-Wali
The text defines Al-Wali as 'al-Malik' (the Owner). Reflect on what it means for Allah to be the Owner of all things.
3
Understand Al-Muta'ali
Al-Muta'ali is said to be like Al-'Ali but with a kind of mubalaghah (emphasis). Consider the difference between 'the High' and 'the Most High'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوالي المالك المتعالي كالعلي ( مع نوع من المبالغة ( البر فاعل البر والإحسان . (التواب يرجع بفضله على عباده إذا تابوا إليه من المعاصي. المنتقم : المعاقب لمن عصاه العفو الماحي للسيئات والمزيل لآثارها من صحائف الأعمال . ( الرؤف : المريد للتخفيف ) على العبيد ( مالك الملك يتصرف فيه. وفي مخلوقاته كما يشاء (ذو الجلال والإكرام كالجليل). قال الآمدي : هو قريب من معنى الجليل

قوله : (قال الآمدي : هو قريب من معنى الجليل قبل التفاوت باعتبار المعنى اللغوي فإن الجليل هو المتصف بالجلال، وذو الجلال هو صاحب الجلال، فالتفاوت والقرب غير مخفي .
Al-Wali (the Protector) is the Owner. Al-Muta'ali (the Supreme) is like Al-'Ali with a kind of emphasis. Al-Barr (the Source of Goodness) is the doer of goodness and kindness. Al-Tawwab (the Acceptor of Repentance) returns with His favor to His servants when they repent to Him from sins. Al-Muntaqim (the Avenger) is the punisher of those who disobey Him. Al-'Afu (the Pardoner) is the one who wipes out evil deeds and removes their traces from the pages of deeds. Al-Ra'uf (the Kind) is the one who wills ease for the servants. Malik al-Mulk (the King of Sovereignty) disposes in it and in His creatures as He wills. Dhu al-Jalal wa al-Ikram (the Possessor of Majesty and Honor) is like Al-Jalil. Al-Amidi said: it is close to the meaning of Al-Jalil.

His saying: 'Al-Amidi said: it is close to the meaning of Al-Jalil, but there is a discrepancy based on linguistic meaning: Al-Jalil is the one described by jalala (majesty), while Dhu al-Jalal is the owner of majesty, so the discrepancy and closeness are not hidden.'
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines nine names. Al-Wali is explained as owner. Al-Muta'ali is like Al-'Ali with emphasis. Al-Barr emphasizes doing good. Al-Tawwab describes acceptance of repentance. Al-Muntaqim punishes disobedience. Al-'Afu wipes out sins. Al-Ra'uf wills ease. Malik al-Mulk has absolute disposal. Dhu al-Jalal wa al-Ikram is compared to Al-Jalil. Al-Amidi clarifies the nuance: Al-Jalil is described by majesty, while Dhu al-Jalal possesses majesty. The footnote further explains the linguistic distinction.

Al-Barr, Al-Tawwab, Al-Muntaqim: Goodness, Repentance, Retribution
Definition of three names: al-Barr, al-Tawwab, al-Muntaqim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of Al-Barr, Al-Tawwab, and Al-Muntaqim as divine names that balance mercy and justice.

Core Idea
Allah is the Source of goodness, accepts repentance, and punishes disobedience.

These three names highlight different facets of Allah's interaction with His creation: He does good, He welcomes back those who repent, and He punishes those who defy Him. Together they show the harmony of mercy and justice in His attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage

4
Understand Al-Muntaqim
Al-Muntaqim is the punisher of those who disobey. This emphasizes divine justice alongside mercy.

الوالي المالك المتعالي كالعلي ( مع نوع من المبالغة ( البر فاعل البر والإحسان . Al-Wali (the Protector) is the Owner. Al-Muta'ali (the Supreme) is like Al-'Ali with a kind of emphasis. Al-Barr (the Source of Goodness) is the doer of goodness and kindness. Al-Tawwab (the Acceptor of Repentance) returns with His favor to His servants when they repent to Him from sins. Al-Muntaqim (the Avenger) is the punisher of those who disobey Him. Al-'Afu and Al-Ra'uf: The Pardoner and the Kind
Definition of two names: al-'Afu and al-Ra'uf.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Allah wipes out sins completely and wills ease for His servants.

These two names focus on Allah's mercy in forgiving sins and providing ease. Al-'Afu goes beyond mere forgiveness to eradicate the traces of sin, while Al-Ra'uf shows His desire to lighten burdens. They inspire hope and trust in Allah's compassion.

Malik al-Mulk and Dhu al-Jalal wa al-Ikram: Sovereignty and Majesty
Definition of two names with a linguistic note on al-Jalil and Dhu al-Jalal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of Malik al-Mulk and Dhu al-Jalal wa al-Ikram, including the subtle distinction between Dhu al-Jalal and Al-Jalil as noted by al-Amidi.

Core Idea
Allah has absolute disposal over creation and possesses all majesty and honor.

These two names emphasize Allah's absolute sovereignty and majestic glory. The text also includes a scholarly note from al-Amidi clarifying the difference between being described by majesty (Al-Jalil) and possessing majesty (Dhu al-Jalal). This nuance enriches our understanding of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the definitions of Malik al-Mulk and Dhu al-Jalal wa al-Ikram, including al-Amidi's note. Notice the linguistic precision.
2
Understand Malik al-Mulk
Malik al-Mulk means the King of Sovereignty who disposes in His kingdom as He wills. Reflect on absolute authority.
3
Understand Dhu al-Jalal wa al-Ikram
This name is compared to Al-Jalil, but al-Amidi clarifies that Al-Jalil is described by majesty, while Dhu al-Jalal is the possessor of majesty.
4
Appreciate the nuance
The note explains the linguistic difference: one is the attribute, the other is the owner of the attribute. Both are valid and beautiful names.

Eight Divine Names: Justice, Sufficiency, and Light
Defines eight names: justice, gathering, wealth, prevention, harm, benefit, light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of eight divine names from the passage, including their linguistic roots and theological significance.

Core Idea
Eight divine names reveal Allah's justice, self-sufficiency, and light.

This passage presents concise definitions of eight names of Allah, each highlighting a different aspect of His nature. From justice and gathering on Judgment Day to self-sufficiency, provision, and light, these names encapsulate key attributes. As you read, notice the linguistic nuances in the definitions, particularly the contrasting roots for 'just' and the simple description of light.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the definitions stand on their own.
Notice the parentheses and contrasting roots in the first definition.
2
Identify each name and its definition
Go through each name one by one. Note how each definition is phrased (e.g., via linguistic root, action, or description).
Al-Darr and Al-Nafi' are paired together.
3
Reflect on the attributes' implications
Consider how these names relate to Allah's actions in the world and to human understanding of His nature.
Think about why Al-Nur is defined as 'apparent by Himself'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقسط : العادل ) من أقسط أي عدل وقسط أي جار، (الجامع أي للخصوم يوم القضاء، الغني : لا يفتقر إلى شيء، المغني : المحسن الأحوال الخلق المانع لما يشاء من المنافع الضار النافع منه الضرر والنفع النور ) . الظاهر بنفسه
Al-Muqsit (the Equitable) is the Just, from aqsata meaning he acted justly, and qasata meaning he acted unjustly (contrasting). Al-Jami' (the Gatherer) is the gatherer of the litigants on Judgment Day. Al-Ghani (the Self-Sufficient) is the one who does not need anything. Al-Mughni (the Enricher) is the one who improves the conditions of creation. Al-Mani' (the Preventer) withholds whatever He wills of benefits. Al-Darr (the Harmer) and Al-Nafi' (the Benefiter) — from Him is harm and benefit. Al-Nur (the Light): the one apparent by Himself.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines the final eight names. Al-Muqsit is explained as just, with a linguistic note on the root. Al-Jami' gathers litigants for judgment. Al-Ghani is self-sufficient. Al-Mughni improves conditions. Al-Mani' prevents benefits. Al-Darr and Al-Nafi' are sources of harm and benefit. Al-Nur is defined as apparent by itself, a simple description. The commentary follows the order.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think harm and benefit come from different sources or that Allah only does good.
Correction: The passage clarifies that both harm and benefit (Al-Darr and Al-Nafi') come from Him alone, as He is the source of all outcomes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Nur is 'apparent by Himself.' If Allah's existence is so evident, why do you sometimes live as if He is absent? What darkness in your life makes you forget that He is the Light? What step will you take today to remove that veil?

Understanding Al-Muhsi: Meanings and Classifications
Commentary on Al-Muhsi with three possible meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different interpretations of the divine name Al-Muhsi and its classification as an additional attribute.

Core Idea
Al-Muhsi has multiple meanings that reveal the depth of divine knowledge and power.

The divine name Al-Muhsi (The Reckoner) is not simply a count of things. Scholars have interpreted it in various ways, linking it to knowledge, speech, or power. This lesson explores the rich commentary provided by the author, showing how each interpretation reflects a different aspect of Allah's perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about Al-Muhsi
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how the author begins with 'صفة إضافية' and then lists three interpretations.
Pay attention to the words 'قيل' which indicate differing scholarly views.
2
Identify the three interpretations
Look for each interpretation: (1) Al-Muhsi as the Knower, (2) as the Informer of numbers, (3) as the Powerful. Note how each relates to a different attribute.
The second interpretation reverts to the attribute of speech, and the third derives from the inability to enumerate blessings.
3
Understand the significance of multiple meanings
Reflect on why a single divine name can have multiple valid interpretations. This shows the richness of divine attributes and the depth of theological reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفة إضافية ( المحصي العالم وقيل : المنبئ عن عدد كل معدود) فيرجع إلى صفة الكلام . وقيل : القادر ، ومنه علم أن لن تحصوه أي لن تطيقوه المبدئ المتفضل بابتداء النعم، المعيد يعيد الخلق بعد هلاكه. المحيي : خالق الحياة المميت : خالق الموت الحي ظاهر مما مر.
An additional attribute: Al-Muhsi (The Reckoner): The Knower, and it is said: The one who informs of the number of everything countable, so it reverts to the attribute of speech. And it is said: The Powerful, and from it one knows that 'you will not enumerate it' means you will not have the capacity for it. Al-Mubdi' (The Beginner): The One who bestows the beginning of blessings. Al-Mu'id (The Restorer): He restores creation after its destruction. Al-Muhyi (The Giver of Life): The Creator of life. Al-Mumit (The Giver of Death): The Creator of death. Al-Hayy (The Living): It is clear from what has preceded.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage defines six divine names: Al-Muhsi, Al-Mubdi', Al-Mu'id, Al-Muhyi, Al-Mumit, and Al-Hayy. For each name, possible meanings and classifications are given. Al-Muhsi is initially described as a relative attribute (sifa idafiyya) and is linked to the attribute of speech or power. Al-Mubdi' and Al-Mu'id relate to creation and restoration. Al-Muhyi and Al-Mumit are explicitly defined as creators of life and death. Al-Hayy is said to be evident from prior discussion.

Key Terms
المحصي
صفة إضافية
صفة الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Muhsi only means 'Reckoner' as in counting.
Correction: It can also mean Knower, Informer, or Powerful, each reflecting different attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which interpretation of Al-Muhsi relates it to the attribute of speech?
A) The Knower
B) The one who informs of the number of everything countable
C) The Powerful
D) The Beginner

Key Takeaway
Al-Muhsi is a multifaceted name that encompasses knowledge, speech, and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Al-Muhsi means that Allah knows every detail of your life and has the power to hold you accountable, what specific sin or habit are you still hiding in the dark, hoping it goes unnoticed? Will you bring it into the light today?
?
The name Al-Muhsi includes Allah's speech informing of every countable thing. When you read the Qur'an, do you truly hear it as the live, personal enumeration of your own deeds? Or have you become comfortable treating it as a distant history?
?
One interpretation says 'you will not enumerate it' means 'you will not have the capacity for it.' In what area of your life are you pretending to be in control, when you are actually incapacitated? What would it look like to surrender that illusion to Al-Muhsi today?

Commentary

Al-Muhsi is initially described as a relative attribute (sifa idafiyya) and is linked to the attribute of speech or power. Al-Mubdi' and Al-Mu'id relate to creation and restoration. Al-Muhyi and Al-Mumit are explicitly defined as creators of life and death. Al-Hayy is said to be evident from prior discussion.

The Divine Names Al-Qayyum, Al-Wajid, Al-Majid, and Al-Ahad
Commentary on four divine names with multiple classifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple interpretations and classifications of four divine names: Al-Qayyum, Al-Wajid, Al-Majid, and Al-Ahad.

Core Idea
Divine names can be classified as essential, action, negative, or relative attributes.

This passage focuses on four divine names from the Islamic tradition, each with multiple interpretations that reveal different aspects of Allah's nature. The author classifies each name into types of attributes (essential, action, negative, relative), showing the depth and nuance in understanding these names. As you read, notice how each name is given alternative meanings and how the classifications shift accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Observe the rhythm and the pattern of 'it is said' (قيل) introducing alternative views.
The passage is dense with technical terms; don't worry about memorizing yet.
2
Examine the first name: Al-Qayyum
Notice that Al-Qayyum is given two interpretations: 'the Everlasting' (essential attribute) and 'the Manager' (attribute of action). Understand that a single name can have different classifications based on the meaning emphasized.
Essential attributes relate to Allah's essence; action attributes relate to His acts.
3
Examine the second name: Al-Wajid
Al-Wajid is interpreted as 'the Rich' (negative attribute, negating need) and alternatively as 'the Knower' (relative attribute) or 'the one with authority' (attribute of action). This shows how one name can span multiple categories.
4
Examine the names Al-Majid and Al-Ahad
Al-Majid is given as a relative attribute. Al-Ahad is explained by reference to earlier discussion, emphasizing uniqueness in essence and attributes. Notice the distinction between Ahad (no composition) and Wahid (no partners).
The distinction between Ahad and Wahid is important to avoid confusion.
5
Reflect on the purpose of classification
Ask yourself why the author lists multiple interpretations and classifications. This demonstrates that divine names are understood comprehensively, not reduced to a single meaning, and that Islamic theology (kalam) systematically categorizes attributes.
This lesson connects to the broader study of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القيوم الباقي الدائم فهو صفة نفسية (وقيل: المدبر) للخلوقات بأسرها فهو صفة فعلية . الواجد الغني أي الذي لا يفتقر فهو صفة سلبية . ( وقيل : معناه العالم الماجد العالي ) فهو صفة إضافية. (وقيل: من له الولاية والتولية) فيكون صفة فعلية . الأحد قد مر تفسيره أي علم ذلك مما سبق أنه في وحدانيته من يمتنع أن يشاركه شيء في ماهيته وصفات كماله، وقد يروى الواحد بدل الأحد ويفرق بينهما فيقال : هو إحدى الذات أي لا تركيب فيه وأوحد الصفات لا مشارك له فيها
Al-Qayyum (The Self-Subsisting): The Everlasting, the Eternal, so it is an essential attribute (sifa nafsiyya). (And it is said: The Manager) of all creatures entirely, so it is an attribute of action (sifa fi'liyya). Al-Wajid (The Self-Sufficient): The Rich, i.e., the one who does not need, so it is a negative attribute (sifa salbiyya). (And it is said: Its meaning is The Knower, The Glorious, The High), so it is a relative attribute (sifa idafiyya). (And it is said: The one who has authority and gives authority), so it is an attribute of action. Al-Ahad (The Unique): Its interpretation has passed, i.e., it is known from what preceded that it is in His oneness such that nothing can share with Him in His quiddity and attributes of perfection, and Al-Wahid is related instead of Al-Ahad, and a distinction is made between them: it is said that He is One (ahad) in essence, i.e., no composition in Him, and Unique (wahid) in attributes, i.e., no partner in them.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage explains four names: Al-Qayyum, Al-Wajid, Al-Majid, and Al-Ahad. For each, it provides multiple interpretations and classifies them as essential, action, negative, or relative attributes. Al-Qayyum is debated between being essential or relational. Al-Wajid is primarily a negative attribute but also interpreted as knowledge. Al-Majid is mentioned as a relative attribute. Al-Ahad is explained through its earlier definition, emphasizing His unique oneness with no composition or partner.

Key Terms
القيوم
الواجد
الماجد
الأحد
صفة نفسية
صفة فعلية
صفة سلبية
صفة إضافية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking Al-Ahad and Al-Wahid are identical in meaning.
Correction: Al-Ahad negates composition (tarkib), while Al-Wahid negates partners. The passage explicitly distinguishes them.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Divine names often have multiple valid interpretations, each leading to a different classification of attributes, and careful distinction between terms like Ahad and Wahid preserves precise theology.

Meanings and Interpretations of As-Samad
Detailed commentary on the divine name As-Samad with multiple interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple interpretations of the divine name As-Samad and how each interpretation relates to attributes of Allah.

Core Idea
As-Samad holds a richness of meanings, each revealing a dimension of Allah's perfection.

In this passage, the commentator explores various interpretations of the sacred name As-Samad. Each interpretation sheds light on a different aspect of Allah's nature—some emphasizing His dominion, others His forbearance, His exaltedness, or His simplicity. Understanding these layers deepens our appreciation of this divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how the commentator presents four interpretations in sequence.
2
Identify the four interpretations
List the four interpretations: Master, Forbearing, High in rank/Invoked, and Solid. Note the classification as relative or negative attribute.
3
Understand the linguistic note
The fourth interpretation includes a note about the substitution of letters (daal for taa). Consider how this relates to the meaning of 'solid' or 'without hollow'.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
Reflect on why a single name can have multiple meanings. This shows the depth of Allah's attributes and the richness of the Arabic language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصمد معناه السيد وهو المالك فيكون صفة إضافية . ( وقيل) معناه الحليم أي الذي لا يستفزه ولا تقلقه أفعال العصاة فتكون صفة سلبية ( وقيل : العالي الدرجة ، وقيل : المدعو المسؤول) الذي يصمد لقضاء الحوائج وعلى التقديرين هو صفة إضافية ( وقيل : الصمد ما لا جوف له أي المصمت فداله مبدلة من التاء وحاصله نفي التركيب وقبول الانقسام.

(And it is said) Its meaning is The Forbearing, i.e., the one whom the actions of the disobedient do not agitate nor disturb, so it becomes a negative attribute. (And it is said: The High in rank, and it is said: The One who is invoked and asked) who is resorted to for fulfilling needs, and in both cases it is a relative attribute. (And it is said: As-Samad is that which has no hollow inside, i.e., solid, so its daal is a substitute for taa, and its outcome is the negation of composition and susceptibility to division.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage exhaustively discusses the name As-Samad, listing four interpretations: 1) Master/Owner (relative attribute), 2) Forbearing (negative attribute), 3) High in rank or the Invoked (both relative), 4) Solid with no hollow (negating composition). Each interpretation is classified as either relative or negative. The final interpretation includes a linguistic note about the substitution of letters.

صفة سلبية

Misconception: Some people limit As-Samad to only the meaning of 'Self-Sufficient' or 'Eternal' without recognizing its other classical interpretations.
Correction: The classical scholars have recorded multiple meanings for As-Samad, including Master, Forbearing, High, Invoked, and Solid. Each meaning is supported by linguistic analysis and classification into relative or negative attributes.

Al-Qadir and Al-Muqtadir: Degrees of Power
Sanusi states that both Al-Qadir and Al-Muqtadir are evident, with the latter more intensive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that both Al-Qadir and Al-Muqtadir are evident attributes of power, with Al-Muqtadir indicating a higher intensity.

Core Idea
Al-Muqtadir is a more intensive form of Al-Qadir.

In this lesson, we explore two of Allah's names that both convey power, but one emphasizes a higher degree. Sanusi highlights the relationship between them, noting that both are evident (zahir) and that the second is more intensive.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for this pair: 'القادر المقتدر كلاهما ظاهر والثاني أبلغ من الأول'. Notice the direct comparison.
The word 'أبلغ' means more intensive or eloquent.
2
Understand the shared attribute
Both names are described as 'ظاهر' (evident). Think about what it means for an attribute to be evident.
3
Grasp the intensity difference
The second name, Al-Muqtadir, is more intensive. Reflect on how this relates to Allah's absolute power.
This follows the pattern of intensification in Arabic names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القادر المقتدر كلاهما ظاهر والثاني أبلغ من الأول المقدم المؤخر يقدم من يشاء ويؤخر من يشاء الأول الآخر لم يزل ولا يزال أي أنه قبل كل شيء وليس قبله شيء وبعد كل شيء وليس بعده شيء فهما صفتان سلبيتان الظاهر المعلوم بالأدلة القاطعة فهو صفة إضافية : ( وقيل : الغالب ) فصفة فعلية من ظهر فلان على فلان أي قهره الباطن المحتجب عن الحواس بحيث لا تدركه أصلاً فيكون صفة سلبية . ( وقيل : العالم بالخفيات الوالي المالك )
Al-Qadir (The Powerful) and Al-Muqtadir (The Prevailing): Both are evident, and the second is more intensive than the first. Al-Muqaddim (The One Who Puts Forward) and Al-Mu'akhkhir (The One Who Puts Back): He puts forward whom He wills and puts back whom He wills. Al-Awwal (The First) and Al-Akhir (The Last): He has never ceased and will never cease, i.e., He is before everything and nothing is before Him, and after everything and nothing is after Him, so both are negative attributes. Az-Zahir (The Manifest): Known by decisive proofs, so it is a relative attribute. (And it is said: The Victorious), so it is an attribute of action, from 'he triumphed over someone' i.e., He overpowered him. Al-Batin (The Hidden): The one veiled from the senses such that they do not perceive Him at all, so it is a negative attribute. (And it is said: The Knower of hidden things, The Guardian, The Owner).
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage covers eight names, often in pairs. Al-Qadir and Al-Muqtadir are both evident with the latter more intensive. Al-Muqaddim and Al-Mu'akhkhir are straightforward. Al-Awwal and Al-Akhir are negative attributes. Az-Zahir has two interpretations: known by proofs (relative attribute) or victorious (action attribute). Al-Batin is negative as hidden from senses, but also interpreted as knowing hidden things (relative). The commentary follows the author's order, noting classifications.

Key Terms
القادر
المقتدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Al-Qadir
B) Al-Muqtadir
C) Both are equal
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadir and Al-Muqtadir both signify power, but Al-Muqtadir denotes a higher intensity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah is Al-Qadir (Powerful) and Al-Muqtadir (Prevailing), which of these two attributes do you need most urgently in your current struggle? Why? And how will you call upon Him using that name today?
?
The difference between Al-Qadir and Al-Muqtadir is intensity. Do you truly believe that Allah's power is not just enough, but overwhelming—more than any problem you face? What would change if you lived with that conviction?
?
If someone were to tell you that Allah is 'just powerful enough', how would you correct them using the words of this lesson? And what sin or doubt in your life would that correct?
