Az-Zahir and Al-Batin: Manifest and Hidden
Sanusi provides two interpretations for Az-Zahir and Al-Batin, with different attribute classifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Az-Zahir and Al-Batin have multiple interpretations, including relative, active, and negative classifications.

Core Idea
Az-Zahir is known through proofs; Al-Batin is hidden from senses.

This lesson covers two names with nuanced meanings. Sanusi presents the primary interpretation and then alternative views. Az-Zahir can be 'known by decisive proofs' (relative attribute) or 'victorious' (action attribute). Al-Batin is 'hidden from senses' (negative) or 'knower of hidden things' (relative).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic
Read 'الظاهر المعلوم بالأدلة القاطعة فهو صفة إضافية : ( وقيل : الغالب ) فصفة فعلية من ظهر فلان على فلان أي قهره الباطن المحتجب عن الحواس بحيث لا تدركه أصلاً فيكون صفة سلبية . ( وقيل : العالم بالخفيات الوالي المالك )'
Pay attention to the parentheses which indicate alternative views.
2
Understand the primary meaning of Az-Zahir
The first interpretation: 'the one known by decisive proofs' – this makes it a relative attribute (sifa idafiyya) because it relates to our knowledge.
3
Understand the alternative meaning of Az-Zahir
The second interpretation: 'the victorious' (al-ghalib) – this is an attribute of action (sifa fi'liyya) derived from overpowering.
4
Understand Al-Batin
Al-Batin is primarily 'hidden from the senses' so that they cannot perceive Him at all – thus a negative attribute. But an alternative says 'the knower of hidden things, the guardian, the owner' which would be relative.
The classification depends on the interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القادر المقتدر كلاهما ظاهر والثاني أبلغ من الأول المقدم المؤخر يقدم من يشاء ويؤخر من يشاء الأول الآخر لم يزل ولا يزال أي أنه قبل كل شيء وليس قبله شيء وبعد كل شيء وليس بعده شيء فهما صفتان سلبيتان الظاهر المعلوم بالأدلة القاطعة فهو صفة إضافية : ( وقيل : الغالب ) فصفة فعلية من ظهر فلان على فلان أي قهره الباطن المحتجب عن الحواس بحيث لا تدركه أصلاً فيكون صفة سلبية . ( وقيل : العالم بالخفيات الوالي المالك )
Al-Qadir (The Powerful) and Al-Muqtadir (The Prevailing): Both are evident, and the second is more intensive than the first. Al-Muqaddim (The One Who Puts Forward) and Al-Mu'akhkhir (The One Who Puts Back): He puts forward whom He wills and puts back whom He wills. Al-Awwal (The First) and Al-Akhir (The Last): He has never ceased and will never cease, i.e., He is before everything and nothing is before Him, and after everything and nothing is after Him, so both are negative attributes. Az-Zahir (The Manifest): Known by decisive proofs, so it is a relative attribute. (And it is said: The Victorious), so it is an attribute of action, from 'he triumphed over someone' i.e., He overpowered him. Al-Batin (The Hidden): The one veiled from the senses such that they do not perceive Him at all, so it is a negative attribute. (And it is said: The Knower of hidden things, The Guardian, The Owner).
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage covers eight names, often in pairs. Al-Qadir and Al-Muqtadir are both evident with the latter more intensive. Al-Muqaddim and Al-Mu'akhkhir are straightforward. Al-Awwal and Al-Akhir are negative attributes. Az-Zahir has two interpretations: known by proofs (relative attribute) or victorious (action attribute). Al-Batin is negative as hidden from senses, but also interpreted as knowing hidden things (relative). The commentary follows the author's order, noting classifications.

Key Terms

الباطن
صفة إضافية
صفة فعلية
صفة سلبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Negative attribute
B) Attribute of action
C) Relative attribute
D) Essential attribute

Key Takeaway
Az-Zahir and Al-Batin have multiple classifications depending on interpretation, showing the richness of divine names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm Allah as Az-Zahir, known through decisive proofs. Have you personally examined those proofs, or are you relying on others? What is one proof you will study this week to strengthen your certainty?
?
You say Allah is Al-Batin, hidden from your senses. Does His hiddenness make you forget that He sees you? What will you do today to live with the awareness that He is watching, even when no one else is?
?
The alternative meaning of Al-Batin is 'Knower of hidden things.' Allah knows every secret thought you have. Is there a secret you are hiding that you need to confess and repent from? What will you do about it right now?

Meanings of Divine Names from Al-Muta'ali to An-Nur
Commentator lists and defines fifteen divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of fifteen divine names from Al-Muta'ali to An-Nur as defined in this classical commentary.

Core Idea
These names reveal how Allah's mercy, justice, and power are expressed in His attributes.

In this passage, the commentator presents concise definitions for fifteen of Allah's beautiful names. Each name highlights a distinct aspect of His nature—from His supreme transcendence to His role as the Light. As you read through, notice how the definitions often contrast or complement one another, building a fuller picture of the Divine.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and find each name
Read the Arabic text slowly, identifying each name and its definition. Try to notice the pattern of definitions.
The passage uses a consistent style of giving a brief explanation for each name.
2
Match the names with their translations
Read the English translation and pair each name with its meaning. Pay attention to specific nuances like hyperbole in Al-Muta'ali.
Some names have additional commentary, like the quote from Al-Amidi.
3
Group the names by theme
Look for names that share a similar attribute—mercy, justice, power, etc. This helps in remembering them.
The grouping is not explicit in the text but aids understanding.
4
Reflect on the final name An-Nur
The passage defines An-Nur as 'the one manifest by Himself,' which is a deeper meaning than physical light. Consider why this definition is unique.
This connects to the misconception about light being physical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتعالي كالعلي مع نوع من المبالغة البر فاعل البر والإحسان . التواب يرجع بفضله على عباده إذا تابوا إليه من المعاصي. المنتقم : المعاقب لمن عصاه العفو الماحي للسيئات والمزيل لآثارها من صحائف الأعمال . الرؤف : المريد للتخفيف على العبيد مالك الملك يتصرف فيه. وفي مخلوقاته كما يشاء ذو الجلال والإكرام كالجليل . قال الآمدي : هو قريب من معنى الجليل المقسط : العادل من أقسط أي عدل وقسط أي جار، الجامع أي للخصوم يوم القضاء، الغني : لا يفتقر إلى شيء، المغني : المحسن الأحوال الخلق المانع لما يشاء من المنافع الضار النافع منه الضرر والنفع النور الظاهر بنفسه
Al-Muta'ali (The Supreme): Like Al-Ali with a kind of hyperbole. Al-Barr (The Source of Goodness): The doer of goodness and beneficence. At-Tawwab (The Oft-Returning): He returns with His bounty to His servants when they repent to Him from sins. Al-Muntaqim (The Avenger): The punisher of those who disobey Him. Al-'Afuww (The Pardoner): The eraser of evil deeds and the remover of their traces from the records of deeds. Ar-Ra'uf (The Kind): The one who intends ease for the servants. Malik al-Mulk (The Owner of All Sovereignty): He disposes in it and in His creatures as He wills. Dhu al-Jalal wa al-Ikram (The Lord of Majesty and Bounty): Like Al-Jalil. Al-Amidi said: It is close to the meaning of Al-Jalil. Al-Muqsit (The Equitable): The just, from aqsata meaning he did justice, and qasata meaning he was unjust. Al-Jami' (The Assembler): i.e., of the adversaries on the Day of Judgment. Al-Ghani (The Self-Sufficient): He does not need anything. Al-Mughni (The Enricher): The one who improves the states of creation. Al-Mani' (The Preventer): The one who prevents what He wills of benefits. Ad-Darr (The Distressor) and An-Nafi' (The Benefiter): From Him is harm and benefit. An-Nur (The Light): The one manifest by Himself.

Commentary
This passage provides brief definitions for fifteen names: Al-Muta'ali, Al-Barr, At-Tawwab, Al-Muntaqim, Al-'Afuww, Ar-Ra'uf, Malik al-Mulk, Dhu al-Jalal wa al-Ikram, Al-Muqsit, Al-Jami', Al-Ghani, Al-Mughni, Al-Mani', Ad-Darr, An-Nafi', and An-Nur. Each is given a straightforward explanation, with occasional notes like the comparison between Dhu al-Jalal and Al-Jalil. The names cover attributes of mercy, justice, power, and transcendence.

The Meanings of Six Divine Names and the Section's Conclusion
Definitions of six divine names with supplication and methodological note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concise definitions of six divine names and the concluding supplication and methodological note.

Core Idea
Each divine name reveals a unique attribute of Allah's perfection and mercy.

This lesson presents the concise definitions of several divine names from the famous hadith of the 99 names. Each definition highlights a particular attribute of Allah, from guiding believers to being the Inheritor of all creation. After the names, we encounter a humble supplication seeking Allah's grace, followed by a note on the author's methodology of following al-Amidi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the sequence: names are defined concisely, then a supplication, then a note.
2
Identify each divine name
Go through the passage and list each divine name mentioned: Al-Muzhir, Al-Hadi, Al-Badi', Al-Baqi, Al-Warith, Ar-Rashid, As-Sabur. Note the brief definition given for each.
Some names like As-Sabur reference a previous discussion.
3
Analyze the definitions
For each name, ask: What attribute does this name emphasize? How does it relate to Allah's perfection?
For example, Al-Hadi emphasizes guidance, Al-Badi' emphasizes creation without model.
4
Reflect on the supplication
Read the supplication after the names. What does it ask for? How does it relate to the names just mentioned?
The supplication seeks goodness, forgiveness, and mercy by the blessing of these names.
5
Understand the methodological note
The commentator states that he followed al-Amidi in the concise interpretation. What does this tell us about the tradition of commentary?
This shows the reliance on earlier authorities and the availability of more detailed works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المظهر لغيره بالوجود من العدم الهادي: يخلق الهدى في قلوب المؤمنين، البديع : أي المبدع فإنه الذي فطر الخلائق بلا احتذاء مثال وقيل : بديع في نفسه لا مثل له الباقي لا آخر له الوارث الباقي بعد فناء الخلق الرشيد العدل وقيل المرشد إلى سبيل الخيرات الصبور الحليم وقد مر فهذه هي الأسماء الحسنى الواردة في الرواية المشهورة نسأل الله ببركتها أن يفتح علينا أبواب الخير ويغفر لنا ذنوبنا ويرحمنا بمنه وكرمه إنه هو الغفور الرحيم الجواد الكريم. ثم إن المصنف تابع الآمدي في تفسير هذه الأسماء على وجه الاختصار تقريباً لفهمها على طلابها فتبعناهما فيه ومن أراد الاستقصاء في ذلك فعليه بالرسائل المؤلفة في تفسيراتها واشتقاقاتها وما ذكر فيها من المعاني المختلفة والأقوال المتفاوتة.
The one who manifests others into existence from nothing. Al-Hadi (The Guide): He creates guidance in the hearts of the believers. Al-Badi' (The Incomparable): i.e., The Originator, for He is the one who created the creatures without following an example, and it is said: He is unique in Himself, having no likeness. Al-Baqi (The Everlasting): There is no end for Him. Al-Warith (The Inheritor): The one who remains after the annihilation of creation. Ar-Rashid (The Rightly Guiding): The just, and it is said: The guide to the path of goodness. As-Sabur (The Patient): The Forbearing, and it has passed. These are the Most Beautiful Names (al-asma' al-husna) narrated in the famous tradition. We ask Allah by their blessing to open for us the doors of goodness, forgive us our sins, and have mercy on us by His grace and generosity; indeed, He is the Oft-Forgiving, the Most Merciful, the Generous, the Noble. Then indeed, the author followed al-Amidi in interpreting these names concisely, to approximate their understanding for his students, so we followed both of them in that; and whoever desires thoroughness in that should consult the treatises authored on their interpretations, derivations, and the various meanings and divergent opinions mentioned therein.

Commentary
This passage defines the remaining names: Al-Muzhir, Al-Hadi, Al-Badi', Al-Baqi, Al-Warith, Ar-Rashid, and As-Sabur. It then states that these are the 'Most Beautiful Names' from the famous hadith, followed by a supplication. The commentary concludes by noting that the author (al-Amidi?) provided concise interpretations, and the commentator followed him; those seeking more detail should consult specialized works.

Answering the Fourth Objection: Miracles Indicate Truth Without Purpose
Al-Jurjani rejects purpose-based indication and offers an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the prophetic miracle's indicative power without requiring a divine purpose, and why this works under both Ash'ari and necessitarian frameworks.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate truth inherently, not because God intended them to.

Al-Jurjani now tackles a key objection: if miracles have no purpose, how can they indicate truth? He argues that God's actions are not goal-oriented in the human sense. Instead, the miracle’s very occurrence on the hand of a claimant creates an intrinsic link to truthfulness, much like blushing necessarily suggests embarrassment. This explanation holds whether one holds God to be a free agent or a necessary cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the source text and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read its literal translation. Let the author's own argument come through before analyzing.
Notice the denial of purpose at the beginning.
2
Identify the central claim and analogy
Al-Jurjani asserts that miracles indicate truth without being created for that purpose. He uses the analogy of blushing to show that a sign can be inherently linked to what it signifies.
The analogy is key to understanding the argument.
3
Consider the two theological frameworks
The argument works under both the view that God is a free agent (qadim mukhtar) and the view that God is a necessitarian cause (mujib). Reflect on how each framework accommodates the inherent indication.
The necessitarian view requires a more indirect causal chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الرابع: إنا لا نقول بالغرض) أي لأنا نقول بأن خلق المعجزة لغرض التصديق لأن أفعاله تعالى عندنا غير معللة بالأغراض ( بل نقول : ( إن خلقها على يد المدعي ( يدل على تصديق له قائم بذاته تعالى كما أن حمرة الخجل تفيد العلم الضروري بحصول الخجلة مع جواز حصولها بدونها أما على القول باستناد الحوادث إلى القادر المختار فظاهر وأما على القول بالموجب فلأنه يجوز أن يحدث شكل غريب سماوي يقتضي تلك الحمرة في ذلك الشخص من غير أن يحصل فيه الخجالة
And as for the fourth: We do not say with purpose) i.e. because we say that creating the miracle for the purpose of confirmation, because His actions in our view are not caused by purposes. But we say: (Its creation on the hand of the claimant (indicates a confirmation of him that is inherent in the essence of God, just as the redness of embarrassment yields necessary knowledge of the occurrence of embarrassment, even though it is possible for it to occur without it. As for the view that events are based on the free chooser, it is clear. As for the view of the necessitarian, it is possible that a strange celestial form occurs that necessitates that redness in that person without embarrassment occurring in him.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This answers the fourth objection that a miracle must have a purpose (gharad) to indicate truth. The response denies that God's actions are based on purposes. Rather, the miracle itself indicates truth by an inherent relationship, analogous to how blushing necessarily indicates embarrassment even though it could occur without it. This works under both the Ash'ari view (qadim mukhtar) and the view of the necessitarians (mujib). For the latter, it is possible that a celestial cause produces the redness without the embarrassment.

Key Terms
غرض
تصديق
حمرة الخجل
قادر مختار
موجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles must be created with the purpose of confirming the prophet.
Correction: Miracles indicate truth inherently, similar to how blushing indicates embarrassment, even if no purpose is involved.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how does a miracle indicate the truth of a prophet?
A) Because God has a purpose for it
B) Because it is inherently linked to truth similar to blushing indicating embarrassment
C) Because people agree on it
D) Because it is impossible otherwise

Key Takeaway
Miracles indicate the prophet’s truth by an inherent relation, not by divine purpose, and this holds under both free-will and necessitarian views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness an undeniable sign of God in your life, do you demand to know its 'purpose' before you surrender? How does this lesson's teaching on inherent indication challenge your need for explanation as a condition for faith?
?
Are you more like those who require a clear purpose from God before believing, or like the one who sees the sign itself as sufficient? What would change if you recognized that His signs carry their own evidence, even when you cannot see the reason?
?
If a miracle were to happen in your presence today, would your heart recognize the truth inherently, or would you reason yourself out of it? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on God's direct signs?

God Cannot Lie: Answer to the Fifth Objection
Al-Jurjani refers to earlier proof on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani refutes the fifth objection by referencing the established impossibility of lying for God from the discussion on divine speech.

Core Idea
God's truthfulness is absolute — a lie is impossible.

This lesson covers al-Jurjani's concise answer to the fifth objection, which suggests that God could support a false claim with a miracle. Rather than repeating the full argument, he refers back to the earlier proof from the topic of divine speech (kalam) where the impossibility of lying was already established.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation. Note the reference to 'the issue of speech'.
This is a very brief answer — al-Jurjani assumes the reader knows the earlier proof.
2
Understand the objection
The fifth objection likely argued that God could create a miracle to support a false prophet, implying that God could lie. Recognize why this is problematic for divine attributes.
Lying contradicts God's truthfulness and perfection.
3
Connect to the earlier proof
Al-Jurjani states that the impossibility of lying has already been established in the discussion on divine speech (kalam). Understand that this earlier proof is sufficient to dismiss the objection.
The proof is not repeated here; the reference carries the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الخامس : فقد مر في مسألة الكلام من موقف الإلهيات امتناع الكذب عليه سبحانه وتعالى .
And as for the fifth: it has passed in the issue of speech from the divine standpoint the impossibility of lying for Him, glorified and exalted.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This very brief answer refers to the earlier discussion in the section on divine speech (kalam) where it was proven that lying is impossible for God. The objection likely suggested that God could create a miracle to support a false prophet, but this is impossible because it would be lying.

Key Terms
كذب
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could lie for a greater good, such as testing faith or protecting a prophet.
Correction: The passage affirms that lying is absolutely impossible for God. His truthfulness is an essential attribute; a lie would contradict His perfection.

Checkpoint

A) In the discussion of miracles
B) In the issue of speech (kalam) from the divine standpoint
C) In the topic of prophethood
D) In the discussion of tawatur

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani resolves the fifth objection by referencing the already-proven impossibility of lying for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Since God cannot lie, every promise in the Quran is absolutely certain. Is there a promise of Allah that you struggle to believe will come true for you? What would change if you treated that promise as already fulfilled?
?
Your speech is a reflection of your character. When you speak, do you treat truth as absolute—as God does—or do you allow yourself 'small' lies? How does the impossibility of lying for God convict you today?
?
If you truly internalized that God's truthfulness is not just a moral choice but an essential impossibility, would you still doubt His word when trials come? Commit today to one specific verse you will trust without reservation.

The Objection: Seemingly Arbitrary Divine Commands
Objection listing divine commands without apparent wisdom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific examples used in the objection to divine commands that appear to lack apparent wisdom.

Core Idea
Some divine commands appear arbitrary, but this does not prove they lack wisdom.

The objector presents several cases where divine commands seem to have no clear benefit or reason. These include actions like uncovering the head, shooting without a target, and kissing the Black Stone, as well as rulings that appear inconsistent. Understanding these examples is crucial to grasping the full force of the objection before we turn to the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage listing the examples and the objector's claim that they lack apparent wisdom. Then read the translation.
Note the list of actions and rulings: uncovering head, shooting, kissing stone, etc.
2
Identify the common thread
Notice that all these rulings seem to have no evident benefit or harm. The objector argues that if there were wisdom, we would see it.
3
Prepare for the answer
Consider why this objection is significant. The answer will show that hidden wisdom exists, even if not apparent.
This connects to the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكشف الرأس والرمي لا إلى مرمى وتقبيل حجر لا مزية له على سائر الأحجار، وكتحريم النظر إلى الحرة الشوهاء دون الأمة الحسناء وكحرمة أخذ الفضل في صفقة و جوازه في صفقتين) كان يبيع مد عجوة جيدة بمدين من عجوة رديئة فإنه ربا حرام وإن باعها بدرهم ثم اشترى به مدين من الرديئة كان حلالاً بلا شبهة (مع استوائهما ) أي الصفقة والصفقتين ) في المصالح والمفاسد ( من جميع الوجوه . ( والجواب بعد تسليم حكم العقل فيه بالحسن والقبح ووجوب الغرض في أفعاله تعالى ( فغايته ) أي غاية ما ذكرتموه ) عدم الوقوف على الحكمة في تلك الصور المذكورة ) ولا يلزم منه عدمها ) في نفس الأمر ( ولعل ) هناك ( مصلحة استأثر الله بالعلم بها على أن في التعبد بما لا تعلم حكمته تطويعاً للنفس الأبية وملكة (قهرها أي تصرف غلبتها الثابتة ( فيما فيه الحكمة وزيادة ابتلاء في التعرض للثواب والعقاب ) يعني أن النفس إذا علمت الحكمة والمصلحة في حكم انقادت له لأجل تلك المصلحة لا لمجرد امتثال حكم مولاها سيدها وكان عندها أنها ذات قوة ورسوخ في العلم فربما صارت بسبب ذلك معجية بنفسها فإذا تعبدت بما لا تعلم حكمته كان انقيادها امتثالاً مجرداً وانكسرت سورتها وإعجابها الثابت لها فيما علمت حكمته وأيضاً في التعبد زيادة ابتلاء في التكليف فإن النفس تأبى عمالاً تعلم مصلحته وكل ذلك حكمة ومصلحة حاصلة في الأحكام التعبدية ومعلومة لنا فلا يلزم خلو تلك الصور عن الحكمة والمصلحة المعتد بها المعلومة.
And uncovering the head and shooting without a target and kissing a stone that has no superiority over other stones, and the prohibition of looking at a free ugly woman while allowing looking at a beautiful slave woman, and the prohibition of taking a premium in one transaction while allowing it in two transactions—he would sell a measure of good ajwa dates for two measures of inferior ajwa dates, that is forbidden usury, but if he sold them for a dirham and then bought two measures of inferior dates with it, it is permissible without doubt—while the two transactions are equal in benefits and harms from all aspects. And the answer, after conceding the judgment of reason regarding good and evil and the necessity of purpose in His actions, may He be exalted, is: the utmost of what you mentioned is the failure to perceive the wisdom in those mentioned cases, and it does not necessitate its absence in reality. Perhaps there is a benefit that God has reserved knowledge of, especially since worshiping without knowing its wisdom involves subjugating the proud soul and establishing mastery over it, i.e., the constant overcoming, regarding that which has wisdom, and increased trial in exposure to reward and punishment. This means that if the soul knows the wisdom and benefit in a ruling, it submits to it for that benefit, not merely to obey the command of its Master and Lord, and it thinks that it possesses strength and firmness in knowledge, so perhaps it becomes self-conceited because of that. So when it worships without knowing the wisdom, its submission becomes pure obedience, and its pride and self-admiration that it had when it knew the wisdom are broken. Also, in worshiping, there is an increase of trial in obligation, for the soul resists actions whose benefit it knows. And all of this is wisdom and benefit present in devotional rulings and known to us, so it is not necessary that those cases are devoid of wisdom and considered benefit that is known.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector lists several divine commands that appear arbitrary or seemingly without wisdom: commands to uncover the head, shoot without a target, kiss the Black Stone, prohibitions that distinguish between a free ugly woman and a beautiful slave girl, and the ruling on usury in one transaction vs. two. The objector claims these lack evident benefit or harm. The answer, after conceding that reason can judge good and evil and that God's actions have purposes, counters that the failure to perceive wisdom does not entail its absence. The answer then provides positive wisdom: such commands train the soul to submit purely out of obedience, breaking its pride and self-admiration, and increase trial, thereby increasing reward. The soul that obeys without seeing the benefit does so purely for the command of the Master, which humbles it. Thus, there is wisdom and benefit in these devotional rulings, and they are known to us, so it is not necessary that these cases are devoid of wisdom.

Key Terms
المصالح والمفاسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That these divine commands are arbitrary and have no purpose.
Correction: The objector merely claims lack of apparent wisdom; the answer will show hidden wisdom.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among the examples given by the objector?
A) Uncovering the head
B) Kissing the Black Stone
C) Fasting on Mondays
D) The ruling on looking at women

Key Takeaway
The objector provides several cases where divine commands appear to lack apparent wisdom, challenging the necessity of purpose in God's actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a divine command in the Qur'an or Sunnah that seems to have no obvious wisdom, what is your immediate reaction? Do you assume there is a hidden benefit you don't see, or do you feel a sense of doubt? Think of one specific command you find difficult to understand. Are you willing to obey it without full comprehension, trusting that the All-Wise never acts without purpose?
?
The objector points to commands like kissing the Black Stone or the different rulings on transactions that appear economically identical. Have you ever found yourself questioning God's wisdom in your own life—perhaps in a trial or a prohibition you didn't understand? How did that affect your faith? Will you commit today to revisiting that doubt with the conviction that God's actions are always perfect, even when His wisdom is hidden from you?
?
These examples challenge the idea that every command has a clear, humanly perceivable benefit. In your daily practice, are there acts of worship you perform mechanically without reflecting on their inner wisdom? What would change if you approached them with the certainty that they are not arbitrary but part of a perfect divine plan? Can you name one act you will strive to perform with deeper mindfulness starting today?

The Answer: Hidden Wisdom in Devotional Rulings
Answer explaining the hidden wisdom in devotional rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reply to the objection and the positive wisdom behind seemingly arbitrary commands.

Core Idea
Hidden wisdom in devotional commands trains the soul in pure obedience and increases trial.

The answer concedes that reason can judge good and evil, but argues that failure to perceive wisdom does not prove its absence. It then explains the profound benefits of such commands: they humble the soul, foster pure obedience, and increase reward through trial. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'والجواب' and its translation. Notice how the author first concedes a point, then responds.
2
Identify the two wisdoms
The passage mentions two benefits of devotional rulings without apparent wisdom: subjugating the proud soul and increasing trial. Locate these in the text.
The first benefit is about breaking pride; the second about increasing reward through trial.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that these wisdoms are known, so the objection is refuted. Consider how this strengthens the case for divine purpose.
The key point: lack of perception does not mean lack of existence.

Source Text

Objection on Levels of Skill
Objection that inimitability is merely relative superiority in eloquence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the Quran's inimitability could be merely relative eloquence and why this is considered invalid.

Core Idea
Relative superiority does not equate to a divine miracle.

Critics argue that every art has degrees of perfection and no fixed limit. They suggest that Muhammad was simply the most eloquent of his time, not performing a miracle. This lesson examines that objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the critic's argument about levels of skill.
Notice the phrase 'وليس لها حد معين' — no fixed limit.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
The argument: if Muhammad was just the most eloquent, then his speech was only relatively superior. If that were a miracle, any superior skill would be a miracle.
The conclusion is deemed 'ضروري البطلان' (clearly false).
3
Understand the refutation
The objection is refuted by showing it would lead to absurd consequences. The commentary explains the interpretation of the phrase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع لكل صناعة مراتب في الكمال بعضها فوق بعض وليس لها حد معين تقف عنده ولا تتجاوزه قوله : ( وليس لها حد معين إلخ فسر الكلام بما يخالف عبارة الكتاب لأنه الأشبه بالحق ولا بد في كل زمان من فائق قد فاق أبناؤها بأن وصل إلى مرتبة من تلك المراتب لم يصل إليها غيره في عصره وإن أمكن أن يفوقه شخص آخر في عصر آخر فلعل محمداً كان أفصح أهل عصره فأتى بكلام عجز عن مثله أهل زمانه ولو كان ذلك معجزاً لكان ما أتى به كل من فاق أقرانه في صناعة من الصناعات في عصر من الأعصار معجزاً وهو ضروري البطلان. وأما مذهب القاضي فلأن ضم غير المعجز إلى مثله لا يصيره معجزاً. وأما الإخبار بالغيب فلوجوه : الأول : أنه جائز كرامة للولي وعلى سبيل الاتفاق أيضاً بلا خرق عادة كما في المرة والمرتين إلا أن يتكرر ذلك الإخبار إلى أن يصير خارقاً للعادة فيصير حينئذ معجزاً ومراتبه غير مضبوطة بعدد معين فكيف يعلم بلوغ القرآن في الإخبار بالغيب مرتبة الإعجاز. الثاني : أنه يقع ذلك الإخبار مكررا من المنجمين والكهنة كما دل عليه التسامع والتجربة وليس بمعجز اتفاقاً. الثالث : أنه يلزم حينئذ أن لا يكون ما خلا عنه من القرآن معجزاً فيخرج أكثر القرآن عن صفة الإعجاز وهو باطل.
The fourth face: Every art has degrees in perfection, some above others, and it has no specific limit at which it stops and does not exceed. His saying: 'And it has no specific limit, etc.' He interpreted the statement in a way contrary to the book's wording because it is more likely to be true. And in every age there must be a surpassing one who surpasses his peers, having reached a level among those degrees not reached by others in his time, although it is possible that another person surpasses him in another age. So perhaps Muhammad was the most eloquent of his contemporaries, so he brought speech that the people of his time were incapable of producing. And if that were a miracle, then everything brought by anyone who surpassed his peers in any art in any age would be a miracle, and this is clearly false. As for the view of al-Qadi (al-Baqillani), because joining a non-miraculous thing to another does not make it a miracle. As for the prediction of the unseen, there are several faces: First: It is permissible as a miracle for a saint (wali) and also by way of coincidence without breaking the norm, as happens once or twice; unless that prediction recurs until it becomes a break of the norm, then it becomes a miracle, and its levels (i.e., the levels of recurrence up to the point of inimitability) are not fixed by a specific number. So how is it known that the Quran has reached the level of inimitability in predicting the unseen? Second: That prediction occurs repeatedly from astrologers and soothsayers, as is established by hearsay and experience, and it is not considered a miracle by consensus. Third: If that were the case, then the parts of the Quran lacking such predictions would not be miraculous, and thus most of the Quran would be excluded from the attribute of inimitability, which is false.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents three more objections against the inimitability of the Quran. The first is the fourth face regarding the levels of skill: every art has degrees of perfection without a fixed limit, and in every age there is a person who surpasses his contemporaries. It is possible that Muhammad was simply the most eloquent of his time, so the Quran was matchless only for that period. But that would not constitute a miracle, because if it did, then anyone who excels in any art would be performing a miracle, which is clearly false. The commentator notes that the phrase 'it has no specific limit' is interpreted differently from the text's literal wording because that interpretation is more plausible. The second objection concerns the view of al-Qadi (al-Baqillani): combining a non-miraculous thing with another does not create a miracle; this refutes the idea that the Quran's inimitability is a composite of several non-miraculous features. The third objection deals with predictions of the unseen: the critics raise three points. First, such predictions can occur as a miracle for a saint or as a mere coincidence without breaking the norm, unless they are repeated many times; but the frequency required to become a miracle is not fixed, so it is uncertain whether the Quran's predictions have reached that level. Second, such predictions are also made repeatedly by astrologers and soothsayers, yet they are not considered miracles. Third, if the miracle were limited to predictions, then the majority of the Quran which does not contain predictions would not be miraculous, which is absurd. The passage presents these objections without immediate refutation.

Key Terms
مراتب الصناعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the objection regarding levels of skill?
A) The Quran is a miracle because of its eloquence.
B) If relative superiority were a miracle, then every superior skill would be a miracle.
C) The Quran has no fixed limit of eloquence.
D) Muhammad was not the most eloquent of his time.

Key Takeaway
The objection based on relative eloquence fails because it would make any superior skill a miracle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a miracle in your own life because you thought it was just a coincidence or someone's exceptional skill? How does this objection force you to reexamine what you consider a true sign from Allah?
?
In what areas of your faith do you settle for being 'good enough' compared to those around you, rather than striving for the absolute standard set by the Quran? Today, commit to one specific change that lifts your practice from relative to absolute sincerity.
?
If the Quran's eloquence were merely relative, your faith would rest on a human achievement. Does your daily reliance on the Quran reflect that you believe it is a timeless miracle, or do you treat it like any other book? What step will you take tonight to show it is different?

Al-Qadi's View on Composition
Al-Qadi's argument against composition of non-miraculous elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Qadi's objection that combining non-miraculous elements cannot produce a miracle.

Core Idea
Adding non-miraculous parts does not create a miracle.

Another objection comes from al-Qadi (al-Baqillani), who argues that if the Quran's inimitability is claimed to be a composite of several non-miraculous features, that composition cannot turn them into a miracle. This lesson explores this concise but crucial point.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully.
This is a single, dense statement.
2
Understand the logic
The argument: joining something non-miraculous to another non-miraculous thing does not change its nature. Therefore, the Quran's inimitability cannot be a mere combination of non-miraculous aspects.
This refutes attempts to explain I'jaz as a composite.
3
Relate to other objections
This objection complements the previous one about levels of skill; both attack the idea that the Quran's uniqueness comes from ordinary human capabilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع لكل صناعة مراتب في الكمال بعضها فوق بعض وليس لها حد معين تقف عنده ولا تتجاوزه قوله : ( وليس لها حد معين إلخ فسر الكلام بما يخالف عبارة الكتاب لأنه الأشبه بالحق ولا بد في كل زمان من فائق قد فاق أبناؤها بأن وصل إلى مرتبة من تلك المراتب لم يصل إليها غيره في عصره وإن أمكن أن يفوقه شخص آخر في عصر آخر فلعل محمداً كان أفصح أهل عصره فأتى بكلام عجز عن مثله أهل زمانه ولو كان ذلك معجزاً لكان ما أتى به كل من فاق أقرانه في صناعة من الصناعات في عصر من الأعصار معجزاً وهو ضروري البطلان. وأما مذهب القاضي فلأن ضم غير المعجز إلى مثله لا يصيره معجزاً. وأما الإخبار بالغيب فلوجوه : الأول : أنه جائز كرامة للولي وعلى سبيل الاتفاق أيضاً بلا خرق عادة كما في المرة والمرتين إلا أن يتكرر ذلك الإخبار إلى أن يصير خارقاً للعادة فيصير حينئذ معجزاً ومراتبه غير مضبوطة بعدد معين فكيف يعلم بلوغ القرآن في الإخبار بالغيب مرتبة الإعجاز. الثاني : أنه يقع ذلك الإخبار مكررا من المنجمين والكهنة كما دل عليه التسامع والتجربة وليس بمعجز اتفاقاً. الثالث : أنه يلزم حينئذ أن لا يكون ما خلا عنه من القرآن معجزاً فيخرج أكثر القرآن عن صفة الإعجاز وهو باطل.

And if that were a miracle, then everything brought by anyone who surpassed his peers in any art in any age would be a miracle, and this is clearly false. Commentary
This passage presents three more objections against the inimitability of the Quran. The first is the fourth face regarding the levels of skill: every art has degrees of perfection without a fixed limit, and in every age there is a person who surpasses his contemporaries. It is possible that Muhammad was simply the most eloquent of his time, so the Quran was matchless only for that period. But that would not constitute a miracle, because if it did, then anyone who excels in any art would be performing a miracle, which is clearly false. The commentator notes that the phrase 'it has no specific limit' is interpreted differently from the text's literal wording because that interpretation is more plausible. The second objection concerns the view of al-Qadi (al-Baqillani): combining a non-miraculous thing with another does not create a miracle; this refutes the idea that the Quran's inimitability is a composite of several non-miraculous features. The third objection deals with predictions of the unseen: the critics raise three points. First, such predictions can occur as a miracle for a saint or as a mere coincidence without breaking the norm, unless they are repeated many times; but the frequency required to become a miracle is not fixed, so it is uncertain whether the Quran's predictions have reached that level. Second, such predictions are also made repeatedly by astrologers and soothsayers, yet they are not considered miracles. Third, if the miracle were limited to predictions, then the majority of the Quran which does not contain predictions would not be miraculous, which is absurd. The passage presents these objections without immediate refutation.

Key Terms
مذهب القاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi's view, what happens when you combine non-miraculous things?
A) They become a miracle if combined sufficiently.
B) They remain non-miraculous.
C) They become partially miraculous.
D) They lose their individual properties.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qadi's objection refutes the idea that the Quran's inimitability is a composite of non-miraculous features.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you read the Quran, do you focus on its individual literary merits or do you seek the direct, life-changing encounter with the Divine that makes it truly inimitable? What would change if you started each recitation expecting a miracle?

Objection from Predictions of the Unseen
Three-fold objection against predictions as evidence of I'jaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-part objection that predictions of the unseen in the Quran are not necessarily miraculous.

Core Idea
Predictions of the unseen are not unique to the Quran and therefore do not prove I'jaz.

The final objection in this section concerns the Quran's predictions of the unseen. Critics raise three points: such predictions can occur as miracles for saints or by coincidence, they are also made by astrologers and soothsayers, and if only predictions were miraculous, most of the Quran would not be miraculous. This lesson unpacks these three arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the three sub-objections
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the three numbered points (الأول, الثاني, الثالث).
The passage lists three distinct arguments.
2
Analyze the first point
Predictions can be from saints (كرامة) or coincidence, and only repeated occurrences become miraculous, but the threshold is unknown.
This challenges the certainty of Quranic predictions being miraculous.
3
Analyze the second point
Astrologers and soothsayers also make predictions, but these are not miracles.
This equates Quranic predictions to non-miraculous sources.
4
Analyze the third point
If only predictions were miraculous, then parts of the Quran without predictions would not be miraculous, which is absurd.
This shows the inconsistency of limiting I'jaz to predictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع لكل صناعة مراتب في الكمال بعضها فوق بعض وليس لها حد معين تقف عنده ولا تتجاوزه قوله : ( وليس لها حد معين إلخ فسر الكلام بما يخالف عبارة الكتاب لأنه الأشبه بالحق ولا بد في كل زمان من فائق قد فاق أبناؤها بأن وصل إلى مرتبة من تلك المراتب لم يصل إليها غيره في عصره وإن أمكن أن يفوقه شخص آخر في عصر آخر فلعل محمداً كان أفصح أهل عصره فأتى بكلام عجز عن مثله أهل زمانه ولو كان ذلك معجزاً لكان ما أتى به كل من فاق أقرانه في صناعة من الصناعات في عصر من الأعصار معجزاً وهو ضروري البطلان. وأما مذهب القاضي فلأن ضم غير المعجز إلى مثله لا يصيره معجزاً. وأما الإخبار بالغيب فلوجوه : الأول : أنه جائز كرامة للولي وعلى سبيل الاتفاق أيضاً بلا خرق عادة كما في المرة والمرتين إلا أن يتكرر ذلك الإخبار إلى أن يصير خارقاً للعادة فيصير حينئذ معجزاً ومراتبه غير مضبوطة بعدد معين فكيف يعلم بلوغ القرآن في الإخبار بالغيب مرتبة الإعجاز. الثاني : أنه يقع ذلك الإخبار مكررا من المنجمين والكهنة كما دل عليه التسامع والتجربة وليس بمعجز اتفاقاً. الثالث : أنه يلزم حينئذ أن لا يكون ما خلا عنه من القرآن معجزاً فيخرج أكثر القرآن عن صفة الإعجاز وهو باطل.
The fourth face: Every art has degrees in perfection, some above others, and it has no specific limit at which it stops and does not exceed. His saying: 'And it has no specific limit, etc.' He interpreted the statement in a way contrary to the book's wording because it is more likely to be true. And in every age there must be a surpassing one who surpasses his peers, having reached a level among those degrees not reached by others in his time, although it is possible that another person surpasses him in another age. So perhaps Muhammad was the most eloquent of his contemporaries, so he brought speech that the people of his time were incapable of producing. And if that were a miracle, then everything brought by anyone who surpassed his peers in any art in any age would be a miracle, and this is clearly false. As for the view of al-Qadi (al-Baqillani), because joining a non-miraculous thing to another does not make it a miracle. As for the prediction of the unseen, there are several faces: First: It is permissible as a miracle for a saint (wali) and also by way of coincidence without breaking the norm, as happens once or twice; unless that prediction recurs until it becomes a break of the norm, then it becomes a miracle, and its levels (i.e., the levels of recurrence up to the point of inimitability) are not fixed by a specific number. So how is it known that the Quran has reached the level of inimitability in predicting the unseen? Second: That prediction occurs repeatedly from astrologers and soothsayers, as is established by hearsay and experience, and it is not considered a miracle by consensus. Third: If that were the case, then the parts of the Quran lacking such predictions would not be miraculous, and thus most of the Quran would be excluded from the attribute of inimitability, which is false.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents three more objections against the inimitability of the Quran. The first is the fourth face regarding the levels of skill: every art has degrees of perfection without a fixed limit, and in every age there is a person who surpasses his contemporaries. It is possible that Muhammad was simply the most eloquent of his time, so the Quran was matchless only for that period. But that would not constitute a miracle, because if it did, then anyone who excels in any art would be performing a miracle, which is clearly false. The commentator notes that the phrase 'it has no specific limit' is interpreted differently from the text's literal wording because that interpretation is more plausible. The second objection concerns the view of al-Qadi (al-Baqillani): combining a non-miraculous thing with another does not create a miracle; this refutes the idea that the Quran's inimitability is a composite of several non-miraculous features. The third objection deals with predictions of the unseen: the critics raise three points. First, such predictions can occur as a miracle for a saint or as a mere coincidence without breaking the norm, unless they are repeated many times; but the frequency required to become a miracle is not fixed, so it is uncertain whether the Quran's predictions have reached that level. Second, such predictions are also made repeatedly by astrologers and soothsayers, yet they are not considered miracles. Third, if the miracle were limited to predictions, then the majority of the Quran which does not contain predictions would not be miraculous, which is absurd. The passage presents these objections without immediate refutation.

Key Terms
الإخبار بالغيب
كرامة الولي
منجمين
كهنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Predictions of the unseen in the Quran are unique and cannot be replicated by anyone else.
Correction: The objection points out that predictions can occur as saintly miracles or coincidence, and also from astrologers and soothsayers, so they are not unique to prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the third point of the objection, what would happen if the miracle were only in predictions?
A) The Quran would be entirely miraculous.
B) Most of the Quran would not be miraculous.
C) Predictions would be more important than other verses.
D) The Quran would lose its inimitability.

Key Takeaway
The objection from predictions argues that predictive content in the Quran does not necessarily prove I'jaz because it can be replicated or is limited in scope.

The Poetry Objection
Critics allege Quran contains poetry despite its denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critics' argument that the Quran contains verses that can be scanned as poetic meters, thereby contradicting its own claim that the Prophet was not taught poetry.

Core Idea
The critic's poetic proof is a deceptive rearrangement, not genuine poetry.

The second face of the objection targets the claim that the Quran lacks contradiction. Critics argue that the Quran itself contains verses that fit poetic meters, despite stating that the Prophet was not taught poetry. This lesson presents their evidence and invites you to evaluate its validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its examples
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the two examples given: the verse about taqwa (65:2-3) and the verse about victory (9:14). See how the critics manipulate them to fit a poetic meter.
Pay attention to the technical terms for poetic meter: فعولن and المتقارب.
2
Understand the argument structure
The critics first claim a contradiction, then support it with specific examples. Notice that they admit the verses are not inherently poetry but become so with slight changes or prolongation of vowels.
This reveals their methodology: artificial alteration, not natural recitation.
3
Ask: Is this a valid contradiction?
Consider whether the critics' manipulation is fair. Does altering the text or reading it in a forced manner prove it is poetry? The commentary will clarify this.
The common misunderstanding is that the examples are actual poetry; they are not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عدم الاختلاف والتناقض فيه مع طوله فلوجوه : الأول : أن فيه تناقضاً لأنه قال : وما علمناه الشعر وفي القرآن ما هو شعر نحو قوله تعالى : ومن يتق الله يجعل له مخرجاً ويرزقه من حيث لا يحتسب فإنه بدون لفظ مخرجاً من بحر المتقارب على وزن فعولن فعولن فعولن فعل ومنه قوله : وأملي لهم إن كيدي متين، نحو قوله : ويخزهم وينصركم عليهم ويشف صدر قوم مؤمنين فإنه إذا أشبع كسرة الميم في ويخزهم وفتحة النون في مؤمنين كان موزونا بلا شبهة سيما أي وفي القرآن ما هو شعر لا سيما إذا تصرف فيه بأدنى تغيير فإنه يوجد فيه شيء كثير على أوزان بحور الأشعار. الثاني : أن فيه كذباً إذ قال : ما فرطنا في الكتاب من شيء وقال : ولا رطب ولا يابس إلا في كتاب مبين ولا شك أنه لا يشتمل القرآن على أكثر العلوم من
And as for the lack of difference and contradiction in it despite its length, [they raise] several faces: First: that there is contradiction in it because He said: 'And We did not teach him poetry' while in the Quran there is poetry, such as His saying: 'And whoever fears Allah, He will make for him a way out and provide for him from where he does not expect' [65:2-3] – for without the word 'makhrajan' it is from the meter of al-mutaqarib on the weight of fa'ūlun fa'ūlun fa'ūlun fa'al. And from it is His saying: 'And I will give them respite; indeed, My plan is firm' [7:183], and His saying: 'And He will disgrace them and grant you victory over them and heal the breasts of a believing people' [9:14] – for if one prolongs the kasra of the mim in 'yukhzim' and the fatha of the nun in 'mu'minīn', then it becomes perfectly metered. Especially, and in the Quran there is poetry, especially if one changes it in the slightest way, then one finds many things on the meters of poetic metres. Second: that there is falsehood in it, since He said: 'We have not neglected in the Book anything' [6:38] and said: 'And no moist or dry but is in a clear Book' [6:59], and there is no doubt that the Quran does not contain most sciences from...
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage presents the objection regarding the alleged lack of contradiction and inconsistency in the Quran. The critics claim two points: First, that the Quran contradicts itself by claiming that the Prophet was not taught poetry, yet it contains verses that can be scanned as poetry. They provide examples: the verse about taqwa (65:2-3) fits the meter of al-mutaqarib if the word 'makhrajan' is removed; the verse about giving respite (7:183) is also poetic; and the verse about disgrace and victory (9:14) becomes metered by prolonging certain vowels. They argue that with slight changes, much of the Quran can be turned into various poetic meters. Second, they claim the Quran makes a false statement by asserting that it contains everything (no moist or dry except in a clear book), while it clearly does not contain most sciences. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the objection is clear. This is the critics' challenge against the Quran's inimitability based on internal inconsistency and overstatement.

Key Terms
الاختلاف والتناقض
الشعر
الوزن
المتقارب
فعولن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran actually contains poetry, as demonstrated by these verses.
Correction: The verses are not poetry in their natural recitation; the critics rely on artificial alterations (omission, prolongation) to force them into poetic meters. The Quran's style is fundamentally different from poetry.

Checkpoint
According to the critics' first objection, what is the basis for claiming contradiction in the Quran?
A) The Quran contains contradictory legal rulings
B) The Quran claims the Prophet was not taught poetry, yet verses can be made to fit poetic meters
C) The Quran lacks predictions of the unseen
D) The Quran is too long to be free of error

Key Takeaway
The critics' poetry objection is based on forced alterations, not the Quran's actual text, and therefore does not constitute a genuine contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an objection against the Quran, do you immediately accept it or do you examine the evidence carefully, as this lesson shows the critic's alteration? Will you commit to investigating each doubt with the same scrutiny?
?
The critics had to force the Quranic verses to fit poetry. Are there areas in your faith where you allow subtle twists of evidence to shape your beliefs instead of letting the Quran speak for itself? What will you do to ensure you approach Allah's word with honesty?
?
If the Quran's own description ('not poetry') can be so easily challenged by superficial manipulations, how firm is your trust in its divine origin? Will you take a step today to strengthen your certainty by studying the Quran's unique linguistic structure?

The Comprehensiveness Objection
Critics allege Quran falsely claims to encompass all knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critics' claim that the Quran falsely asserts comprehensive knowledge while lacking many sciences.

Core Idea
The Quran's claim of comprehensiveness is spiritual, not an exhaustive inventory.

The second face of the objection targets the Quran's claim to contain everything. Critics point to verses like 'We have not neglected in the Book anything' and 'no moist or dry but is in a clear Book' to argue that the Quran is incomplete. This lesson examines their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage beginning with 'الثاني : أن فيه كذباً' and understand the critics' accusation of falsehood.
They cite two Quranic verses: 6:38 and 6:59.
2
Identify the verses used
Find the exact verses cited: 'ما فرطنا في الكتاب من شيء' (6:38) and 'ولا رطب ولا يابس إلا في كتاب مبين' (6:59). Note that the critics interpret 'al-kitāb' as the Quran.
These verses are often interpreted more broadly by Muslim scholars.
3
Consider the counter-argument
Ask yourself: Does the Quran claim to contain every detail of every science? Or does it mean it contains all guidance? The commentary will address this.
The objection is incomplete in the source; the response would clarify the intended meaning.

The Principle of Non-Necessity in I'jaz
Logical principle: failure in one aspect does not imply failure in all.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a lack of inimitability in one aspect of the Quran does not undermine its overall miraculous nature.

Core Idea
One aspect's weakness does not destroy the whole's miracle.

This lesson centers on a crucial logical principle for defending the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz). The passage argues that just because the Quran may not be miraculous in one specific aspect, it does not follow that it is not miraculous in all aspects or even any single aspect. This principle refutes a common objection and is illustrated with a clear analogy from poetry and prose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the structured logical progression.
2
Identify the main principle
Find the sentence that states the core principle: if not miraculous in one aspect, it does not necessarily mean not miraculous in all aspects.
Look for the phrase 'لا يلزم' (does not necessitate).
3
Understand the reasoning
The passage gives a reason: rulings (ahkam) can differ across aspects. Reflect on what that means.
Example: poetry vs. prose skill.
4
Examine the example
The example of a person skilled in poetry but not prose shows that ability in one does not imply ability in all. How does this support the principle?
5
Apply to the Quran
The author concludes that the Quran is miraculous in its eloquence (balaghah). Connect the principle to this affirmation.
The principle protects the overall i'jaz from partial critiques.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس إذ لم يكن معجزاً بالنظر إلى أحد من وجوه الإعجاز ما بيناه بعينه يلزم أن لا يكون معجزاً بجملتها ولا بجملة منها بل ولا بواحد منها لا بعينه لجواز اختلاف الأحكام في هذه الأمور الأربعة وكأي من بليغ يقدر على النظم أو النثر ولا يقدر على الآخر ولا يلزم من القدرة على أحدهما القدرة على الجميع وليس كل ما ثبت لكل واحد يثبت للكل من حيث هو كل ولا لجملة من الأفراد المتعددة كعشرة مثلاً وكذلك قد يختلف حكم الواحد مطلقاً ومعيناً، فإن الأول قد يكون متعين الثبوت دون الثاني خذ هذا الذي ذكرناه وإنا نختار أنه معجز ببلاغته.
And if it is not miraculous with respect to one of the aspects of inimitability that we explained specifically, it does not necessarily follow that it is not miraculous with respect to all of them, nor with respect to a group of them, nor even with respect to any single one of them without specification, because the rulings concerning these four matters may differ. And how many a skilled person is capable of poetry or prose but not capable of the other, and capability for one does not necessitate capability for all. And not everything that is established for each individual is established for the whole as such, nor for a group of multiple individuals like ten for example. Similarly, the ruling for the absolute individual and the specific individual may differ, for the first might be necessarily established without the second. Take this that we have mentioned. And we choose that it is miraculous in its eloquence.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument defending the Quran's inimitability (i'jaz). It begins by stating a general principle: if a thing is not miraculous in one specific aspect, it does not entail that it is not miraculous in all aspects, a group of aspects, or even any single aspect whatsoever. The reason given is that the rulings (ahkam) regarding these aspects can differ. The author supports this with an example: a person skilled in poetry may not be skilled in prose, and skill in one does not imply skill in all. Similarly, properties of individual items do not necessarily transfer to the whole or to a group. The conclusion is that the Quran's lack of miraculousness in some aspect does not negate its overall miraculous nature, and the author affirms that the Quran is indeed miraculous in its eloquence (balaghah).

Key Terms
إعجاز
بلاغة
وجوه الإعجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If any aspect of the Quran is not miraculous, then the entire Quran loses its inimitability.
Correction: The passage argues that lack of i'jaz in one aspect does not negate i'jaz in all aspects; rulings can differ, and properties of parts do not necessarily apply to the whole.

Checkpoint
What principle does the passage use to argue that the Quran's inimitability is not lost if it fails in one aspect?
A) The aspects of inimitability are all identical in ruling.
B) The ruling for one aspect necessarily applies to all.
C) Rulings for different aspects may differ, and properties of individuals do not automatically transfer to the whole.
D) The Quran is only miraculous in its entirety, not in parts.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is not negated by a lack of miraculousness in any single aspect; it is established by its overall eloquence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed the Quran’s overall message because a single aspect (e.g., a historical statement or a legal ruling) seemed unimpressive to you? How does this lesson’s principle challenge you to re-evaluate your approach to the Quran? Will you commit to seeking its overarching eloquence rather than judging by isolated parts?
?
Think of someone you know who struggles in one area of their faith (e.g., prayer or character). Does that weakness make you undervalue their other strengths in the deen? How does the logical principle from this passage (failure in one aspect does not negate overall worth) compel you to change your view of them? What specific action will you take to correct this?
?
This lesson argues that the Quran’s inimitability is established by its eloquence, not by every aspect being miraculous. Do you rely on the Quran’s beauty and depth in your own life, or do you focus on criticisms of specific details? What would it mean for you to truly trust in the Quran’s overall miraculousness, starting today?

Answer to the Second Objection: Meaning of 'al-Kitab'
Answers an objection about the word 'kitab' in two verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two ways the term 'al-Kitab' in disputed verses can be interpreted to resolve apparent contradictions.

Core Idea
The word 'kitab' can mean the Preserved Tablet or the Qur'an without conflict.

In theological debates, opponents sometimes cite verses that seem contradictory. Here, the author provides two reasonable interpretations of the word 'kitab' that preserve consistency. This lesson unpacks those interpretations and shows how careful reading removes the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Notice that the author gives two distinct answers to the objection. List them clearly.
The first interpretation refers to the Preserved Tablet, the second to the Qur'an with a qualification.
3
Understand the resolution
For each interpretation, understand why it removes the apparent contradiction between the verses.
The Preserved Tablet ensures no inconsistency; the Qur'an's generality is restricted to needed parts.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider the broader principle: words can have multiple valid meanings, and context determines which is intended.
This is an example of 'general intended as specific' (العموم والخصوص).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثانية: إن المراد بالكتاب المذكور في الآيتين هو اللوح المحفوظ فلا إشكال، أو المراد القرآن لكن أريد بالعموم الخصوص بما يحتاج إليه في أمر الذين، إذ القرآن مشتمل على جميع أصوله.
And the answer to the second [objection]: that what is meant by 'the Book' mentioned in the two verses is the Preserved Tablet, so there is no difficulty; or what is meant is the Qur'an, but generality is intended as particularity regarding what is needed for the matter of those [people], since the Qur'an includes all its principles.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage provides two possible answers to an objection about the word 'al-kitab' appearing in two verses that seemed contradictory or problematic. The first answer is that 'al-kitab' refers to the Preserved Tablet, which contains all divine decrees, thus no inconsistency. The second answer is that even if it refers to the Qur'an, the general term is used to mean specifically those parts that are needed for the people in question, because the Qur'an contains all principles of guidance. The objection is thereby resolved.

Key Terms
الكتاب
اللوح المحفوظ
العموم والخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an contradicts itself.
Correction: The term 'al-Kitab' can refer to the Preserved Tablet or the Qur'an contextually, thus resolving apparent contradictions.

Checkpoint
What is the first interpretation of 'al-kitab' given in the answer to the second objection?
A) The Qur'an.
B) The Preserved Tablet.
C) The Torah.
D) The Gospel.

Key Takeaway
The term 'al-Kitab' is used flexibly in the Qur'an, either referring to the Preserved Tablet or the Qur'an itself, and apparent contradictions are resolved through careful interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems unclear or potentially contradictory, do you immediately assume a problem with the text, or do you trust that there is a valid interpretation? How does your immediate reaction reflect your relationship with the Quran? What step can you take this week to cultivate a deeper trust in its consistency?
?
The author offers two valid interpretations that both resolve the objection. Are you comfortable with the idea that multiple correct understandings of the same divine word can exist? Does your pride or certainty ever prevent you from considering alternative perspectives? Commit to studying a verse you think you already understand with the humility to learn something new.
?
How often do you read the Quran with attention to rhetorical devices like 'general intended as particular'? This lesson shows that careful linguistic analysis removes confusion. What is one verse you have previously found difficult, and how might applying this principle change your understanding? Will you take time this week to look up its tafsir?

Proof from the Teaching of the Names
Second argument: Teaching of Names proves Adam's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic teaching of names to Adam establishes his superiority over the angels, and how a potential objection about angelic knowledge is refuted.

Core Idea
Adam's knowledge of all names proves his superiority over angels.

This passage presents the second of four arguments for Adam's superiority. It draws on the Quranic account where Adam is taught all the names, while the angels confess their ignorance. The author then addresses a counterargument that angels have greater knowledge from the Preserved Tablet, showing why the verse itself settles the matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic passage
Read verses 2:31-32, noticing the contrast between Adam's knowledge and the angels' admission.
The verses are the core of the argument.
2
Understand the argument
Recognize that the verse establishes Adam's complete knowledge and angels' limited knowledge. The author also cites verse 39:9 to show knowledgeable are better.
3
Examine the objection
Consider the claim that angels have vast knowledge from the Preserved Tablet and experience. See how the author refutes it by pointing to the verse's purpose.
The refutation is concise but clear.
4
Connect to the lesson
Reflect on why this argument is considered a proof of superiority: direct divine teaching and the value of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني قوله تعالى : وعلم آدم الأسماء كلها ﴾ [البقرة : ٣١]) إلى قوله : قالوا سبحانك لا علم لنا إلا ما علمتنا ﴾ [البقرة : ٣٢ ] ، فإنه يدل على أن آدم علم الأسماء كلها ولم يعلموها ( والعالم أفضل من غيره لأن الآية سبقت لذلك، ولقوله تعالى : من قل هل يستوي الذين يعلمون والذين لا يعلمون ﴾ [الزمر : ۹] .
قوله : ( لأن الآية سيقت لذلك فيه دفع لما يقال أن لهم علوماً جمة أضعاف العلم بالأسماء لما شاهدوا من اللوح المحفوظ، وحصولاً في الأزمنة المتطاولة بالتجارب والأنظار المتوالية فلا يلزم بماذكر أفضلية آدم عليه السلام منهم ووجه الدفع ظاهر.
Second: His saying, exalted be He: 'And He taught Adam the names, all of them' [Al-Baqarah 2:31] to His saying: 'They said: Glory be to You, we have no knowledge except what You have taught us' [Al-Baqarah 2:32]. For it indicates that Adam was taught all the names and they were not taught them. And the knowledgeable is better than others because the verse was brought for that, and because of His saying: 'Say: Are those who know and those who do not know equal?' [Az-Zumar 39:9]. His statement: 'Because the verse was brought for that' contains a refutation of what is said: that they (the angels) have immense knowledge many times greater than knowledge of names because of what they witnessed from the Preserved Tablet, and acquisition over long periods through experiments and successive observations, so the superiority of Adam over them does not follow from what was mentioned. And the refutation is clear.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument: God taught Adam all the names, while the angels admitted they only know what He taught them. This shows Adam's knowledge exceeded theirs. The author supports this by citing the Quranic verse 'Are those who know and those who do not know equal?' The commentary then addresses a potential objection: that angels have vast knowledge from the Preserved Tablet and long experience, so the specific knowledge of names might not prove overall superiority. The response is that the verse is explicitly structured to demonstrate Adam's superiority, and the objection is dismissed as weak.

Key Terms
الأسماء
علم
اللوح المحفوظ
أفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because angels have vast knowledge from the Preserved Tablet and long experience, their knowledge surpasses Adam's, negating his superiority.
Correction: The verse (2:31-32) is specifically structured to demonstrate Adam's superiority by highlighting his direct knowledge of all names, and this overrides any other knowledge the angels may have.

Checkpoint
What does the author use to refute the claim that angels have greater knowledge than Adam?
A) The fact that angels learned from the Preserved Tablet.
B) The verse 'Are those who know and those who do not know equal?'
C) The angels' confession in the Quran itself.
D) The difficulty of human worship.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic teaching of names establishes that knowing the names of things is a form of knowledge that elevates Adam above the angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you stand before Allah, will you be among those who know His names and act upon them, or among those who confess ignorance like the angels? Which name of Allah do you need to learn more deeply starting today?
?
The angels admitted they only know what Allah taught them. When was the last time you humbly admitted you were ignorant about a matter of faith, and sought knowledge rather than pretending to know? How can you make this a regular habit?
?
If your superiority in the sight of Allah depends on knowledge of His names, what are you actively doing to learn them? Write down one specific name you will memorize and reflect on this week.
